==PAGE:1==
Martin Stampe Thomsen, s110071
Reduction of Cross-Polarization
for Oﬀset Reﬂector Antennas
Master’s Thesis, January 22, 2017
==PAGE:2==
==PAGE:3==
iii
Abstract
The oﬀset reﬂector antenna is used as mobile land terminal in commercial
satellite communication.
The conﬁguration oﬀers a high directivity, but the
missing symmetry generates a cross-polar component in the secondary radiation
pattern. The polarization of the antenna is dependent on the physical location
of the terminal and a rotation of the feed is used to align the polarization with
that of the satellite.
Satellite operators have strict requirements on polarization discrimination.
A solution to the cross-polar radiation is desired which is physically disconnected
from the feed. This project investigates a solution using a two dimensional screen
located 5 mm in front of the feed and 4 cm from the focal point.
A MATLAB script is developed to calculate the secondary radiation pattern
from a current in the focal region using physical optics. The script is veriﬁed
against results from GRASP with success. The locations of desired currents in
the screen are located by analyzing the electric ﬁeld distribution in the focal
plane from a plane wave incident on the antenna at boresight.
A physical
realization using bend dipoles scatterers are proposed and investigated.
The superimposed currents take the form of Hertzian dipoles and should
be located symmetrically about the symmetry plane at two distinct locations.
Using two such dipoles equal in amplitude, oriented in opposite direction and 90°
delayed compared to the incident ﬁeld, a reduction in the cross-polar component
of 11 dB is obtained. However, a solution to two orthogonal polarizations cannot
generate the desired dipole moment of 650 µA m when illuminated by a feed
radiation 4π W.
Implementations using 1 to 6 bend dipole scatterers are simulated for one
linear polarization only, where an improvement of maximum 6.4 dB is achieved.
When the mutual coupling betwen the screen and the feed horn is included in
a full-wave simulation in GRASP, the improvement disappears completely.
Mutual coupling is concluded as a signiﬁcant factor, and cannot be disre-
garded in designing a screen. Combined with the fact that no two dimensional
structure was identiﬁed performing the desired operation, the general approach
employed in the project is concluded as questionable.

Preface
This project has been carried out over the past 5 month in a joint collaboration
between the Technical University of Denmark, Electromagnetic Systems group and
the Antenna R&D department at the Cobham SATCOM Lyngby division. The thesis
is a mandatory part of the DTU master program and has a workload of 30 ECTS
points.
I would like to send a special thanks to my main DTU superviser professor Olav
Breinbjerg, the company superviser Ulrich V. Gothelf, and the secondary DTU ad-
visor Ph.D. candidate Michael Forum Palvig. All have been a tremendous source of
inspiration throughout the project period and has continuously provided invaluable
insights.
A number of people has helped make this project possible, including Milos Pavlovic
at the WIPL-D support team who’s help has been essential in understanding the errors
and ﬁle formats of WIPL-D, and William Yatman for his support and companionship
during the time we spent sharing the oﬃce. To all the employees of the Cobham
SATCOM Antenna R&D department, the canteen and to Cobham SATCOM in gen-
eral, I would like to thank all of you for the oppotunity of writing my thesis at the
Lyngby oﬃce.
Technical University of Denmark, Lyngby, January 2017
Martin Stampe Thomsen

List of Terms
Term
Description
EFIE-MoM
The process of solving an electric ﬁeld integral equation using the
method of moments.
EIRP
Deﬁned by IEEE as: In a given direction, the gain of a transmitting
antenna multiplied by the net power accepted by the antenna from
the connected transmitter. [2].

Key parameter of the parabolic reﬂector antenna. Focal length to
reﬂector diameter of the projected aperture.

==PAGE:21==
1
Introduction
Communication and connectivity has become increasingly important over the past
two decades, with an internet connection now required even in remote locations and
communication must be delivered fast and instantly. In order to accommodate the
increasing demand for bandwidth, modern communication systems must follow tight
requirements for the antenna radiation pattern and purity. In the case of commercial
satellite communication, land terminals are directed at closely spaced satellites, where
the overall performance depends on limiting the impact of one signal to all others by
specifying the radiation pattern, polarization and spectral purity of user terminals.
The oﬀset parabolic reﬂector has found applications in a wide range of applications
including satellite communication systems due to the high directivity. An example
is shown in Fig. 1.1(a). The oﬀset conﬁguration oﬀers signiﬁcant advantages over
its symmetrical counterpart, due to the lack of aperture blocking by feed and struts
along with a high isolation between feed and reﬂector [3].
However, the asymmetrical geometry inherently creates cross-polarization. When
illuminated by a perfectly linear polarized source, the curvature of the reﬂector and
the lack of symmetry in one plane results in signiﬁcant cross-polarization. This dis-
advantage may be eﬀectively mitigated using the concept of a matched feed [3,5–8],
where the higher order modes are generated in the horn to compensate. The added
ﬁeld elements must be stationary relative to the reﬂector, why the horn must remain
ﬁxed in position. When the oﬀset reﬂector is used in mobile land terminals, the feed is
usually rotated to align with the polarization vector of the satellite. An implementa-
tion of the conjugate matched feed for this application must include some additional
(a) Oﬀset reﬂector antenna
(b) Screen in front of horn
Figure 1.1. The oﬀset reﬂector antenna. (a) Graphic showing a vehicle roof mount
oﬀset reﬂector antenna [4] and (b) displays the idea of a screen in front of the horn.

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
feature adding complexity and costs.
The purpose of this thesis is to investigate the possibility of lowering the cross
polarization of the antenna, by inserting some sort of two dimensional screen between
the feed and the reﬂector. The principle is shown in Fig. 1.1(b), where the blue grid
is the screen and the cylindrical box is the feed horn. The screen must perform the
same operation as the higher order modes but allow the feed horn to be rotated
independently.
Chapter 3 investigates why the oﬀset conﬁguration inherently creates cross-polar-
ization, and further describes why the matched feed concept works and how it may be
applied in the case of a two dimensional screen. Chapter 4 develops a simulation tool
in MATLAB to calculate the far-ﬁeld radiation using physical optics. The program
oﬀers better ﬂexibility than commercial tools, and is veriﬁed against results from
TICRA’s GRASP software. Chapter 2 outlines the needed theory to describe the
problem, develop the simulation tool, and to investigate a possible solution. A screen
is designed in Chapter 5 and tested in Chapter 6. Chapter 7 wraps up the project in
a conclusion and comments on possible future work.
Due to time constraints, the investigation is limited to a single transmit frequency
of 14.125 GHz and a speciﬁc conﬁguration currently used by Cobham SATCOM,
namely the EXPLORER 8100 utilizing a 1 m reﬂector, focal length of 0.8 m and a
tilt angle of 37° [4]. The screen is placed 5 mm in front of the horn aperture, where
a dielectric rain cover is currently located. The EXPLORER 8100 is subject to the
requirements speciﬁed for earth stations by the satellite operator Eutelsat, which are
used to relate the achieved reduction.
==PAGE:23==
2
Theory
This chapter documents the understanding of the theory used in this work, beginning
with an introduction to the oﬀset reﬂector antenna and its associated geometry, fol-
lowed by general antenna theory, and a description of the used numerical techniques
and software employed. The theory is outlined in general terms and applied to the
speciﬁc of this work using references in the succeeding chapters.
2.1
The Oﬀset Parabolic Reﬂector
The class of parabolic reﬂector antennas has found applications in a wide range of
areas due to the high directivity and easy analysis. In theory, the standard axisym-
metric parabolic reﬂector oﬀers zero cross polarization along with good side lobe
suppression. The feed is in all cases placed at the focal point, which for the axisym-
metric reﬂector is right in the middle of the collimated beam. The phenomenon is
called aperture blocking and results in a signiﬁcant scattering of the radiated energy
leading to a deterioration in gain, side lobes levels and polarization purity, along with
a coupling between the reﬂector and the feed itself [3].
The major advantage of the oﬀset parabolic reﬂector is the removal of the feed
from the main beam and is obtained by using only a subsection of the paraboloid,
mitigating all of the above disadvantages [3]. Unfortunately, by using a subset of the
paraboloid only one plane of symmetry remains. As will be shown in Section 3.2, the
lack of symmetry is what essentially enables the radiation of a cross polarized signal.
When illuminated by a perfectly co-polarized source, the oﬀset reﬂector will give rise
to cross polarization in far ﬁeld cuts outside the symmetry plane.
This work relies heavily on modeling the performance of the oﬀset parabolic re-
ﬂector, why the following section will outline the formulas and document an under-
standing of the geometry.
Geometry and Deﬁnition
The parabolic reﬂector is a truncated part of the body of resolution of a parabola,
where the parabola is deﬁned as the intersection of a plane and a cone [9].
Fig-
ure 2.1(a) illustrates the conﬁguration clearly showing the the ellipse generate by the
cone and plane intersection, with Fig. 2.1(b) deﬁning the geometry parameters: α is
the cone half angle, β is the smallest angle between the cone axis the plane, and δ is
the distance from the vertex to the plane along the cone axis. Only the cases with δ
positive and β < 90° are considered.
The intersection will form a hyperbola when α > β, a parabola with α = β and a
ellipse when α < β. All possess the geometrical property of having two focal points
along the axis of symmetry. For the hyperbola, any ray originating from one focal
3
==PAGE:24==
4
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
(a) Overview
(b) Side view
Figure 2.1. The intersection of a cone and a plane. Modiﬁed from [9]. Since α < β
the interconnection forms a ellipse.
point and diﬀracted by the surface, will act as if is was launched at the other. For
the ellipse, the rays will collimate in phase at the second focal point.
The collimation of rays are of obvious interest to the antenna engineer.
The
parabola may be seen as the limit of the ellipse with a focal point at inﬁnity, thus all
rays emanating from a plane wave propagating along the axis of symmetry, will col-
limate in phase at one point in space. Other examples are Cassegrain and Gregorian
dual reﬂector antennas, utilizing truncated surfaces of the hyperbola and the ellipse
respectively.
A paraboloid is obtained when rotating the parabola around its axis. Thus in the
right handed coordinate system of Fig. 2.1 and letting z = 0 at the intersection with
the parabola, the paraboloid surface is given by
z = x2 + y2
4F
,
(2.1)
where x,y and z are the coordinates of the surface and F is the focal length of the
parent parabola, i.e. the distance along the z-axis from the coordinate system origin
to the nearest focal point.
The oﬀset reﬂector is quite amusingly deﬁned as yet a cone intersection, in this
case between the cone and the paraboloid surface, where the cone vertex coincide
with the focal point. The projecting of the intersection onto the xy-plane as shown
in Fig. 2.2 will represent a true circle [3]. It is seen that θ∗is the cone half angle, θ0
is the oﬀset angle and D is the diameter of the projected aperture. In order to avoid
aperture blocking is it noted that θ0 must be greater than θ∗. It may be convinient
to measure the amount of oﬀset as the distance from the projected aperture to the
symmetry axis of the parent paraboloid:
Doﬀset = F tan(θ0 −θ∗).
(2.2)
The parameters D, F, θ0, and Doﬀset thus completely deﬁnes the oﬀset reﬂector
geometry, and are those given for the reﬂector conﬁguration in this work.

Antenna Theory
In the following, and in the rest of this work, time harmonic ﬁelds and currents are
assumed with the factor ejωt omitted everywhere. j will denote the imaginary unit,
ω is the frequency in radians, and t is the time.

General Considerations & Scatterers
An antenna is a device designed to radiate or receive electromagnetic radiation. In
deﬁning the properties of an antenna, is it not needed to describe both the trans-
mitting and receiving properties. It follows from the reciprocity theorem, that one
is equivalent to the other.
However, some concepts are easier to visualize in one
mode, why the operation of the antenna will change in the following without a loss
of generality.
Radiation Pattern.
The most general property of an antenna is the radiation
pattern, deﬁned by IEEE [2] as the spatial distribution of a quantity that characterizes
the electromagnetic ﬁeld generated by an antenna. I.e., the amount of for instance
the electric ﬁeld, power or a normalized power transmitted in diﬀerent directions. A
radiation pattern may be shown in three dimensions as in Fig. 2.3 or more commonly
in two dimensions as done in Fig. 3.3.
For the oﬀset reﬂector antenna, one may distinguish between the radiation pattern
of the feed alone, or the combined radiation pattern of the feed and reﬂector. The
former is denoted as the primary and the later as the secondary radiation pattern
(following among others [3, 12]1). When the primary source has negligible inﬂuence
on the secondary pattern, the later may be calculated based on the reﬂector currents
alone.
1IEEE deﬁnes the secondary radiator as that portion of an antenna having the largest radiating
aperture..., which is probably where the term originates.
==PAGE:27==
2.2. ANTENNA THEORY

For a isotopic radiator, being only possible in theory, the directivity in all direction
equals unity. The directivity may thus be interpreted as the gain compared to this
theoretical antenna with the losses of both being equal.
Field Regions.
The area in which an antenna radiates may be divided into separate
parts with diﬀerent properties. The transition from one part to the other is smooth
and graduate. The immediate surroundings to the antenna is labeled the reactive
near-ﬁeld. As an observer moves further away from the antenna, she passes through
the radiating near-ﬁeld (Fresnel) and ends in the far-ﬁeld region (Fraunhofer). The
names given in parenthesis originates from the ﬁeld of optics with no diﬀerence in
interpretation.
The reactive near-ﬁeld is by IEEE deﬁned as that portion of the near-ﬁeld region
immediately surrounding the antenna, wherein the reactive ﬁeld predominates. The
reactive parts consists of evanescent waves carrying only reactive power.
The ra-
diating near-ﬁeld is deﬁned as the region ...wherein the angular ﬁeld distribution is
dependent upon distance from the antenna. That is, the radiation pattern may not
be calculated independent of r and thus a radiation pattern carries no meaning. In
the far-ﬁeld however, the angular ﬁeld distribution is essentially independent of the
distance from a speciﬁed point in the antenna region. The radiation pattern is thus
a far-ﬁeld quantify. For a receiving antenna in this region, the wavefront may be
assumed that of a plane wave and the ﬁeld components orthogonal to each other with
no radial component.
Antenna Gain, Eﬃciency and Power Gain.
The gain of an antenna is essen-
tially its directivity taking losses into account:
G(θ, φ) = e0D
(2.13)
where e0 is the total antenna eﬃciency with values between zero and unity. The
gain of an antenna is interpreted as the ampliﬁcation compared to a lossless isotropic
antenna and, like the case of directivity, may be divided into the gain of diﬀerent
polarizations.
The total eﬃciency may be broken into the product of multiple terms including
reﬂection, polarization, conduction, and dielectric eﬃciency. The last two are diﬃcult
to separate in practice and are not important to this work, since we are mainly
concerned with lossless materials.
The reﬂection mismatch eﬃciency is given by
1 −|Γ|2, where Γ is the input reﬂection coeﬃcient of the antenna with impedance ZA

for a conjugate matched antenna is all available power transfered to the antenna and
radiated (or vice versa). With the deﬁnition of gain in Eq. (2.13), we have actually
deﬁned the gain as a combination of both the partial gain and the realized gain, but
the distinction is not pursued further here.
The available power, Pav, of an antenna in receiving mode is a function of the
power density of the incident ﬁeld and the eﬀective area, Aem,
Pav(θ, φ) = Aem(θ, φ)|Wav|,

eﬃciency, otherwise the power becomes the realized power gain. When the direction
is not speciﬁed, the maximum eﬀective area is implied.
The Thévinin Equivalent for Antennas and Scatterers.
To describe the be-
havior of a circuit driven by a linear antenna, one may apply the Thévinin equivalent
as shown in Fig. 2.44. As is evident on the ﬁgure, the antenna is substituted by a
circuit component representing at the terminals a and b. The equivalent circuit only
mirrors the physical behavior of the antenna at the terminals, and should not be used
to calculate current or voltages of the circuit components inside the antenna itself.
In Fig. 2.4 the antenna is seen connected to a load ZT = RT + jXT and itself
represented by a loss resistance RL, a Thévinin voltage VT, a radiation resistance Rr
and reactance XA. The previously referenced antenna impedance, ZA in Eq. (2.14),
is for a lossless antenna given by
ZA = Rr + jXA.
(2.17)
The values of Rr and XA are frequency dependent, and may be calculated in a
number of diﬀerent ways. Only simple structures such as dipoles lend themselves
to analytical solution, whereas more complicated structures may have the antenna
impedance found from a numerical solution to an integral equation.
A scatterer is an object interacting with electromagnetic waves and disrupts the
ﬁeld. According to the physical equivalent theorem, outlined later in this chapter,
4The representation may fail for some antennas, since the dielectric and ground plane losses
cannot be represented by a resistor in series with the radiation radiation resistance [11, Sec. 2.13].
==PAGE:30==

(a) Receiving antenna
(b) Thévenin equivalent
Figure 2.4. An antenna in receiving mode connected to a load in (a) with the corre-
sponding Thévinin equivalent shown in (b). Illustration from [11].
the perturbation of the ﬁeld may be represented as a superposition of the original
ﬁeld and that "generated" or scattered by the scatterer.
In the equivalent circuit representation of above, one may interpret a scatterer as
an antenna loaded by a short. This is useful when the scatterer has the form of an
already known type of antenna as the impedance is thus readily available. A dipole
shorter than the corresponding resonant dipole will excite a capacitative behavior,
which may be used to control the phase of the current on the scatterer. From the
interpretation of the scatterer as a shorted antenna, it is expected that the phase of
current will be a function of dipole length, when the incident ﬁeld is assumed being
in phase with the driving voltage VT.
Mutual Coupling.
When more than one antenna is present in close proximity, the
input impedance at the terminals of any of the antennas becomes complicated than
the above5, and thus change the behavior of the circuit. Without going into details,
the presence of objects in the vicinity may signiﬁcantly alter the performance of the
antenna. The exact eﬀect is diﬃcult to include in a closed form exspression and often
only included when needed by a numerical calculation.
In order to simplify the design of a system, it may be advantageous to ignore
mutual coupling based on a qualiﬁed guess or by mere hope to be quantiﬁed in a
later stage. Factors to consider include the physical separation of objects, geometries
and relative orientation.
The Deﬁnition of Cross Polarization.
In order to reference the polarization of
a ﬁeld as co or cross-polarized, is it necessary to deﬁne what is understood by these
terms. The choice is somewhat arbitrary and may be deﬁned in numerous ways, some
being more useful than others [3]. IEEE deﬁnes co-polarization as the polarization

the antenna is intended to radiate and cross-polarization simply as the orthogonal

Ludwig’s 1st deﬁnition is somewhat trivial, since the unit vectors simply follow
that of the given components in a Cartesian coordinate system:
ˆaco,1L =ˆx,
(2.20)
ˆacr,1L =ˆy,
(2.21)
for a source primarily oriented in the ˆx-direction and with maximum radiation along
the ˆz-axis.
According to Ludwig in [12], the 3rd deﬁnition main advantage is when describing
a radiation pattern, but carries no physical meaning when describing source currents.
Ludwig’s 1st deﬁnition is clearly not valid when evaluation radiations patterns, but
is the obvious choice when deﬁning source currents. Thus, whenever a co or cross-
==PAGE:32==

CHAPTER 2. THEORY
polarized component is referenced in the following, the deﬁnition will depend on the
said component being a source or a radiated component.
It is noted, that a perfect source according to deﬁnition 1 may indeed lead to cross
polarization in the far-ﬁeld following deﬁnition 3. In order to get a perfect relationship
between feed and radiation, one could use the 2nd deﬁnition, which however entrails
a series of other issues.
One source conﬁguration that leads to zero cross polarization is the Huygens’
source described in the next section. An other is a circular feed with equal E- and
H-plane amplitude and phase patterns [12], much like that of corrugated horn.
Huygens’ Source.
To an antenna engineer, the ideal antenna would be one radi-
ating only the desired polarization in one direction. One particular source called the
Huygens’ source, albeit theoretical, comes close to the ideal characteristic. The Huy-
gens’ source consists of two crossed Hertzian dipoles, one electric and one magnetic
deﬁning a plane. The dipoles interfere constructively in one direction perpendicular
to the plane and destructively in the other, thus procession a good directivity [14].
Furthermore, the radiated ﬁelds from the source consists only of a co-polarized com-
ponent according to Ludwig’s 3rd deﬁnition [12].
Given the dipole moment of the electric dipole, pe, the dipole moment of the
magnetic dipole is given by pm = ηpe [14]. Or equivalently, the radiated power of the
two isolated from each other most be equal.

Electromagnetic Theorems and Principles
The following section shortly summarize the important theorems and principles with
reference to [11,15].
Image Theory
Image theory describes how a inﬁnite plane of either a Perfect Electric Conductor
(PEC) or a Perfect Magnetic Conductor (PMC) eﬀects the radiated ﬁelds from a
source placed in its vicinity. The PEC and PMC shorts the electric and magnetic
ﬁelds respectively, giving rise to reﬂections at the surface. It may be shown that the
reﬂection is equivalent to an image of the original current source at the same distance
below the surface having a prescribed direction, whose radiation adds to that of the
original source. Figure 2.7 shows the orientation of the images relative to the sources.
==PAGE:37==
2.2. ANTENNA THEORY
17
The resulting ﬁeld is obviously only valid in the half plane of the original source,
but in creating the image, the PEC/PMC half plane is converted into the same media
as the source region. As stated above, having an unbound homogeneous media is a
requirement when using the radiation integrals.
The resulting radiation from the
original and image source is usually found by applying classic array theory. When
the original source is not speciﬁed as an independent current, the image theory is
unable to provide an exact solution since mutual coupling is ignored.
In practice, nothing has an inﬁnite extent, however the approximation has been
shown to yield good results for object which are electrically large, i.e. large in com-
parison the the wavelength.
Boundary Conditions
The general boundary condition for the ﬁelds between any two media is [15]
Js = −ˆn × (H2 −H1),
(2.54)
Ms = ˆn × (E2 −E1),
(2.55)
where the subscript indicates the corresponding region, and the surface normal, ˆn,
is deﬁned pointing from region 2 into region 1. For a media of ﬁnite conductivity,
the surface currents Js and Ms will be zero, while a PEC or a PMC allows a ﬁnite
current by zeroing the magnetic or electric ﬁelds respectively.
Surface Equivalence Theorem
The surface equivalence theorem is a generalization of Huygens equivalence principle
and based on the uniqueness theorem. In short, the uniqueness theorem states that
the ﬁelds in a lossy medium is uniquely deﬁned inside of a closed boundary by the
sources inside the region and knowledge of either the tangential electric or magnetic
ﬁeld, or by parts of one and parts of the other, over the entire boundary.
Given that sources only exist inside the volume deﬁned by the boundary, it thus
follows that the ﬁelds outside may be known by the tangential ﬁeld alone.With refer-
ence to Fig. 2.8, the surface equivalence theorem thus states that the arbitrary sources
J1 and M1 inside the volume V1 radiating the ﬁelds E1 and H1 is equivalent to the
ﬁctitious current Js and Hs on the closed boundary S when evaluating the ﬁelds in
V2. The equivalent currents are radiating in unbound space, and (E1, H1) may be
evaluated using the radiation equations in Section 2.2.
The ﬁctitious currents must satisfy the boundary conditions and are given by
Js = ˆn × (H1 −H),
(2.56)
Ms = ˆn × (E −E1).
(2.57)
==PAGE:38==
18
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
(a) Actual problem
(b) Equivalent model
Figure 2.8. The surface equivalence theorem. (a) illustrates the actual sources J1
and M1 inside V1 while (b) illustrates the equivalent sources Js and Hs placed at
the surface S radiating the exact same ﬁelds E1 and H1 outside of V1. Illustration
from [11].
If we choose the ﬁeld inside V1 to be zero, the currents reduce to
Js

H=0
= ˆn × H1,
(2.58)
Ms

E=0
= −ˆn × E1.
(2.59)
This form of the equivalence principle is known as Love’s equivalence principle. Given
the uniqueness theorem, we may change the constitutive parameters of V1 with those
of any desired material as long as the tangential ﬁelds remain unchanged. Alterna-
tively, one could argue that the ﬁeld inside V1 is deﬁned as zero, and the constitutive
parameters thus cannot possibly have any inﬂuence. Substituting either a PEC or
a PMC will eliminate the electric or magnetic current respectively, which may or
may not make the equivalent problem easier to solve, since the source is no longer
radiating in unbound space rendering the radiation integrals useless.
Physical Equivalent and Physical Optics
The physical equivalent method addresses the scattering of incident ﬁelds in a simple
homogeneous media from a PEC particle as shown in Fig. 2.9(a). Physical optics
approximates the surface current and further converts the scatterer into a media
with the same constitutive parameters as the surrounding media.
Consider the ﬁelds (Ei, Hi) generated by the sources (J, M) in an unbound region
with constitutive parameters (ϵ1, µ1). In the presence of a scatterer with properties
(ϵ2, µ2), the ﬁelds from (J,M) will be change and may be written as
H = Hi + Hs,
(2.60)
E = Ei + Es,
(2.61)
==PAGE:39==
2.2. ANTENNA THEORY

Figure 2.9. PEC scatterer occupying the volume V1 with the surface S and corre-

where (Es, Hs) is the change from the homogeneous case, and may be interpreted as
the scattered ﬁeld from the particle. The ﬁeld transmitted to the scatterer is denoted
(Et, Ht) which inside a PEC are zero.
In order for (Es, Hs) and (Et, Ht) to be solutions to the original problem, the
ﬁelds must be a solution to the linear Maxwell’s equations alone. By subtracting
the incident ﬁeld (Ei, Hi), an equivalent problem emerge subject to the boundary
conditions:
Mp = ˆn × (−Ei −Es),
(2.62)
Jp = ˆn × (Hs + Hi),
(2.63)
where the fact that (Et, Ht) = (0, 0) is used. Furthermore, due to the uniqueness
theorem, one may zero the magnetic current and write Eqs. (2.62) and (2.63) as
Jp = ˆn × (Hs + Hi) = ˆn × H
(2.64)
0 = ˆn × (−Ei −Es)
(2.65)
where the later may be written as
ˆn × Es = −ˆn × Ei
(2.66)
The above equivalent is termed the physical equivalent and shown in Fig. 2.9(b).
Since (−Ei, −Hi) is a solution to Maxwell’s equations in the media of (ϵ1, µ1), the
equivalent problem is one where Jp radiates in a homogeneous media, and we may
use the radiation equations of Eqs. (2.46) to (2.53).
==PAGE:40==
20
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
Physical Optics.
Unfortunately, H remains an unknown, and the equivalent prob-
lem has not made the exact solution to the problem any easier. Now assuming the
surface being locally ﬂat, one may use the image principle and write
Jp = 2ˆn × Hi
(2.67)
where the tangential component of H is simply interchanged with twice that of the
incident ﬁeld Hi. Equation (2.67) is denoted the Physical Optics (PO) principle and
applies only to the part of the scatterer in direct line of sight of the source with the
current set to zero over the remaining part of the surface. The approximation clearly
has a discrepancy at the boundary of the visible and invisible region violating the
continuity equation. This is sometimes mitigated by introducing a line source around
the edge of the reﬂector, but is not pursued further in this work [3].
Electric Field Integral Equation
An electric ﬁeld integral equation (EFIE) is usually combined with the method of
moments (MoM) to solve the problem of the scatterer using boundary conditions
inforced on the electric ﬁeld. The combined procedure is referenced as EFIE-MoM.
An integral equation is one where the unknown appears inside the integral as oppose
to an integral expression where the unknown appears outside.
Method of Moments.
Following the derivation in [11, Sec.
8.4] and [18], the
general equation of which to apply MoM is given by
F(g) = h,
(2.68)
where h is a known excitation, F is a known linear operator and g is some unknown
continuous quantity. To reduce complexity, a one dimensional distribution is assumed
with g and h being functions of x. Using the linearity property however, g is approx-
imated by a number of known basis or expansion functions with diﬀerent coeﬃcients:
g(x) ≈
N
X
n=1
angn,
(2.69)
where gn is the nth basis function and an is the corresponding unknown complex
coeﬃcient. The basis functions may be formed in a number of diﬀerent ways. From
simple piecewise constants (the subdomain approach) to more complicated function,
one being increasing order of polynomials spanning the entire integration domain
(entire domain bases). Figure 2.10 gives two such examples from [11]. Note that the
piecewise sinusoidal of Fig. 2.10(c) is somewhat distorted, but serves the purpose of
illustration a more complex basis function.

Dirac’s delta function. Thus the error is forced to zero at N diﬀerent values of x,
but with an unbound error at all other locations. When the test function is chosen
as wm = gn, the test procedure is referred to as Galerkin’s method 7.

EFIE-MoM in WIPL-D and GRASP.
WIPL-D [20] and GRASP [21] are two
software solutions with the capability of performing EFIE-MoMs.
Both software
packages uses a one and two-dimensional version of Eq. (2.78), enforced on a local
coordinate system of each wire and plate respectively [19, 22]. This is due to the
volume Ωreducing to a single line or surface. The solution in local coordinates are
then transformed to the main 3D system from which a proper 3D solution emerge.
This is speciﬁcally addressed in Appendix B for a plate in WIPL-D, where a MATLAB
script is developed.
Figure 2.11 shows the representation of the local coordinates used in both software
suites: Wire currents are assumed to have only a none-zero axial component while
the plates have two (independent of possible curvature of the plate, which is included
in the bilinear quadrilateral representation). Only GRASP is able to use higher order
wires and plates than 2 and 4 respectively.
The expansion function used are in both cases entire-domain polynomials of in-
creasing order over each wire or plate, as the technique is superior in terms of the
number of variables needed to obtain a certain degree of convergence [19, Sec. 2.2.2].
When two plates or wires are joined together, the current leaving one is required to
enter the other, and thus relating each subsystem, or instantiation of Eq. (2.78), to
the others.
==PAGE:44==
24
CHAPTER 2. THEORY
As testing functions, both WIPL-D and GRASP uses the Galerkin’s method pre-
viously mentioned.
2.3
Optimization
Following the deﬁnitions in [23, Ch. 22.], optimization is the process of minimizing
or maximizing some function f(x1, ..., xn) = f(x). The function to be optimized is
reference as the objective function8 and the inputs the control variables. Only the
case of a minimum is addressed here9. The control variables may be constrained or
unconstrained, where the former deﬁnes the allowed values of x and may drastically
limit the solution domain.
The objective function has a local minimum at x = x0 when
f(x) ≤f(x0)
(2.79)
hold only the immediate vicinity of x0, and a global minimum when Eq. (2.79) is
valid for all x.
A well known local optimizer is that of the gradient method, which uses the gradi-
ent of f to search for the minimum. Given an initial guess x1, the algorithm calculates
the next guess, x2, as
x2(t) = x1 −ζ∇f(x1)
(2.80)
such that
w′(ζ) = 0,
w(t) = f(x2(ζ)).
(2.81)
In other words, ζ is the distance along the direction of the largest inﬁnitesimal decrease
of f, and chosen such that the new guess is expected to be a stationary point (and
thus possibly a minimum).
Figure 2.12(a) displays an example with f(x) = x2
1 +3x2
2, and is seen to approach
the minimum at (0, 0). It is evident that the local optimizer may completely miss the
global minima only dependent on the initial guess. Furthermore, the above scheme
requires detailed information about the objective function not available to a numeric
solver. MATLAB has a number of built-in optimization functions, utilizing both local
and global search algorithms. Figure 2.12(b) shows the a comparison between a local
(red) and global (green) solver in MATLAB. It it seen, that the global solver searches
for the global minimum by polling all over the solution space of x, and not relying
on a single initial guess and subsequent gradients [24]. A detailed description of the
algorithm used from the MATLAB library is out of scope of this work.
Given no information about behavior of a function, one is forced to use a much
slower global solver over a local solver. One such example is ga.m, MATLAB’s Genetic
Algorithm, which generates an initial random population of N guesses, computes
the objective function, and uses the results to generate a new population based on
mutations or combinations of the former and new random samples.
8MATLAB refers to the objective function as the ﬁtness function.
9Finding the maximum of a function is equivalent to ﬁnding the minimum of −f(x).
==PAGE:45==
2.3. OPTIMIZATION
25
(a) Gradient method
(b) Global vs. local search
Figure 2.12. Examples of optimization routines. (a) shows the 4 steps of the gradient
method using f(x) = x2
1 + 3x2
2 where the initial guess x0 = (2, 2.5), and (b) shows
ga.m as green vs. the local solver fmincon.m in red. (b) is from [25]
When a global minima is located, one could use a local solver to quickly converge
to the bottom.
A convergens to the actual minimum is however not guarantied,
and the solution thus only provides a upper bound to the minimum of the objective
function.
This work uses a combination of ga.m and the local solver fminsearch.m. Whether
this is the fastest or best procedure is unknown, but it performed the desired oper-
ation. As a small side note one may add, the so-called no free lunch-theorem rules
out the idea of an optimum algorithm without knowing the nature of the objective
function: any improved gained in one class of problem is oﬀset by slower performance
in an other [26].
The General Algorithm further has the advantage, that it allows mixed-integer
problems, where one or more control variables are integers only. This is used when
selecting matrix indexes in the discretized source grid.

==PAGE:47==
3
The Source of Cross Polarization
This chapter intends to clarify the source of the cross polarization generated by an
oﬀset parabolic reﬂector antenna, what the requirements of the project are and how
we intend to quantify the problem. The chapter begins by outlining the coordinate
systems used throughout the project along with other objects and geometrical refer-
ences.
3.1
Coordinate System deﬁnitions
Two right handed Cartesian and one spherical coordinate system are shown in Fig. 3.1
and used to describe the antenna conﬁguration. A main coordinate system (x, y, z)
is used where ˆx coincides with the rotational axis of the parent paraboloid and its
origin at the intersection of ˆx-axis and the paraboloid. The second coordinate system
(x′, y′, z′) is chosen with origin at the focal point of the paraboloid and ˆy′ being
the axis of the cone deﬁning the oﬀset parabolic reﬂector. ˆz′ is chosen such that
ˆz′=ˆz. It is seen that ˆx′ and ˆy′ are rotated θ0 around the ˆz-axis compared to ˆx and
ˆy respectively.
The main coordinate system (x, y, z) will be used to describe the geometry of
the reﬂector itself, while (x′, y′, z′) is used to describe the geometry of the horn and
surrounding structures along with corresponding ﬁeld components. (x′, y′, z′) will be
denoted the oﬀset coordinate system, since it is oﬀset relative to the main coordinate
system.
The far-ﬁeld radiation is deﬁned in the spherical system (r, θ, φ) with the origin
coinciding with that of (x, y, z). The angle θ is deﬁned as the angle to ˆx and φ the
angle to ˆy towards the ˆz-axis in the ˆyˆz-plane.
Given the coordinates (x′, y′, z′), the corresponding coordinates in (x, y, z) are
found using a rotation matrix and an oﬀset corresponding to the focal length:
x = sin(θ0)x′ −cos(θ0)y′ + F,
(3.1)
y = cos(θ0)x′ + sin(θ0)y′,
(3.2)
z = z′.
(3.3)
When a vector is given relative to the (x′, y′, z′) system, a prime is added to the
name and components. Given the vector components (A′
x, A′
y, A′
z), the corresponding
components in the (x, y, z) system is calculated by omitting the oﬀset:
Ax = sin(θ0)A′
x −cos(θ0)A′
y,
(3.4)
Ay = cos(θ0)A′
x + sin(θ0)A′
y,
(3.5)
Az = A′
z.
(3.6)
The deﬁnition of the spherical coordinate system is somewhat unusual, but is a
consequence of the reﬂector boresight not being aligned with the ˆz-axis. In order to

perform far-ﬁeld cuts through boresight, θ must be deﬁned relative to the boresight
axis, ˆx. To convert spherical coordinate into (x, y, z), we may use the normal formulas
when substituting x →y, y →z and z →x:
x = cos(θ),
(3.7)
y = sin(θ) cos(φ),
(3.8)
z = sin(θ) sin(φ).
(3.9)
The radial component, r, is omitted above since the spherical coordinate system is
used only for the far-ﬁeld radiation formulas utilizing unit vectors with |r| = 1 .
The symmetry plane of the oﬀset reﬂector antenna is that given by ˆyˆx, ˆy′ˆx′ or ˆrˆθ
when φ = 0. When a far-ﬁeld cut is made in the symmetry plane, is will be designated
an elevation or vertical cut. The symmetry plane will further be designated as the
==PAGE:49==
3.2. THE SOURCE OF CROSS POLARIZATION
29
Figure 3.2. The EXPLORER 8100 mounted on top of a vehicle from [4]. The desig-
nations of vertical and horizontal are clear from the orientation to the ground.
vertical plane. When a far-ﬁeld cut is made in the perpendicular plane (ˆxˆz), it is
designated an azimuth or horizontal cut, and the plane labeled the horizontal plane.
Due to personal preference, the terms vertical and horizontal will be the primary
designators. Imagine the antenna mounted on top of a vehicle as shown in Fig. 3.2
with boresight directed parallel to the ground. The elevation cut will correspond to
a cut vertical to the road, while an azimuth cut is horizontal or parallel to the road.
Consider a plane wave incident on a PEC plane tilted relative to the Poynting
vector. The reﬂected ﬁeld will be polarized in the same plane, but have a tilt angle
corresponding to the tilt of the PEC. The same is the case for the oﬀset reﬂector
antenna, where a ˆy-polarized plane wave is supposed to translate into a ˆx′-polarized
ﬁeld. Both are regarded as co-polarized and both are in the vertical plane. We will
thus reference this ﬁeld as being ˆv-polarized, with ˆv being a pseudo unit vector.
Likewise with a ˆz polarized wave, which translates into a ˆz′ wave at the focal point:
ˆh = ˆz.
3.2
The Source of Cross Polarization
This section investigates the source of the radiated cross polar component by investi-
gating the current on the reﬂector when illuminated by perfectly co-polarized source,
and addresses the justiﬁcation of the matched feed, in which the radiated ﬁeld from
the horn is conjugate matched to the focal plane ﬁeld. The later is used to propose
a alternative approach of achieving the match using the 2D screen.
Investigating the Reﬂector Currents
Using the physical optics MATLAB program developed in this work and outlined in
Chapter 4 and the geometry constants in Table 3.1, the secondary radiation pattern

plot clearly shows a cross polar component in cuts outside of the symmetry plane.

the cross polar radiation is evident. Plotted is the co- and cross-polar current com-

projected aperture with x = 0). The co-polar component is the strongest and sym-
metric about the symmetry plane as expected based on the orientation and placement
of the feed. The radiation from the co-polarized current will thus add constructively
on axis, and slightly less constructive outside resulting in a lowered directivity.

|Jcx| (mA m−1)

Figure 3.5. Reﬂector surface. (a) plots the ˆx coordinate and (b) surface normal in the
ˆyˆz-plane and the amplitude of the cross-polar current. Note how the length changes
when the normal starts to diﬀer from ˆx. Calculated using the MATLAB program.
The cross-polar component is seen to have a change of sign at the transition from
one side of the symmetry plane to the other. With the opposite phase relationship,
the cross-polarized current adds destructively and completely cancel for an observer
in the symmetry plane. Outside however, a cross-polar components will emerge since
the phase relative to the observation point is no longer exactly out of phase.
In the above analysis, the radial x component of the surface current was ignored.
The said component is none zero over the entire reﬂector, and thus contributes to a
cross-polar component outside boresight. According to [12] the cross-polar current is
the dominant mechanism over the radial component for observation angles close to
boresight, and thus the x component may safely be disregarded.
The main diﬀerence between an axisymmetric and an oﬀset parabolic reﬂector
is thus the missing symmetry of the oﬀset conﬁguration. In the axisymmetric, any
cut would correspond to a cut in the symmetry plane thus leaving only a co polar
radiated ﬁeld.
The Source of the Cross-Polar Current.
Figure 3.4 showed how the cross-
polar current component increases with the distance to the symmetry plane of the
reﬂector. The amplitude over the entire projected aperture is plotted in Fig. 3.5(b)
with an overlay of the surface normal in the ˆyˆz-plane. Figure 3.5(a) displays the
actual coordinates of the oﬀset reﬂector.
The cross polar component on the reﬂector is given by
Jz = nxHy −nyHx,
(3.10)
where the subscript indicate the component of the normal ˆn and feed H-ﬁeld evaluated
at the reﬂector. In the symmetry plane, only a z component of the magnetic ﬁeld
exist for a co-polarized feed (Ludwig 3. deﬁnition) polarized in the vertical plane.
Both Hy and Hx will grow the further from the symmetry plane the ﬁeld is evaluated,
==PAGE:52==
32
CHAPTER 3. THE SOURCE OF CROSS POLARIZATION
and are approximately of the same amplitude (albeit in a complicated manner taking
direction and directivity of the feed into account). The sign of Hy and Hx both change
in the transition from one side of the symmetry plane to the other, and thus change
the sign of Jz. The value of nx dominates that of ny, resulting in |nxHy| > |nyHx|
by a factor of 3.
One obvious way of lowering the cross-polar radiation would be to increase the
ﬂatness of the reﬂector, by simply moving it further way from the feed. Given a
constant area of the projected aperture, this would lead to a lower angle of the cone
deﬁning the oﬀset reﬂector and thus in eﬀect simultaneous decrease the values of Hy
and Hx. A decreased curvature translates to increasing the F/D ratio of the reﬂector.
Oﬀsetting the balance of the two terms subtracted in Eq. (3.10) in order for them
to cancel is possible. However, increasing the ratio of ny to nx requires larger y values
of the reﬂector, making the surface area larger for the same antenna area and the
structure more bulky. Oﬀsetting the ratio of Hy to Hx would correspond to changing
the feed, and most likely mirror the result of the conjugate matched feed outlined
in the next section. By tailoring the magnetic ﬁeld in a way not zeroing both Hy
and Hx, one would have to assume that the two right hand side terms of Eq. (3.10)
are of the same shape in order to cancel. This is not the case, especially not for a
ˆh-polarized feed.
A similar analysis would emerge if the feed had been polarized along the horizontal
axis. In this case, one would have to minimize
Jy = nzHx −nxHz,
(3.11)
of which nzHx is the dominant factor, and the conclusion of increasing the F/D ratio
again applies.
When the feed is circularly polarized, the phenomena translates into a beamsquint,
where the direction of maximum directivity no longer coincide for right and left hand
polarized signals [3].
Focal Region Electric Field
Multiple investigations has been performed into the source of the cross polarizations.
In [27], the electric ﬁeld in the focal plane and along the cone axis has been derived,
based on a plane wave incident at boresight.
This analysis was extended in [28]
to include small scan angles around boresight for the focal plane electric ﬁeld only.
Both are performed using the physical optics principle of Section 2.2. Due to the
time constrains on the project, this study limits the initial study to that of boresight
incidence given by D. J. Bem in [27].
Focal Plane Fields.
When a ˆv-polarized (ˆy) plane wave is incident on the reﬂector,
the ﬁeld distribution of the electric ﬁeld in the focal plane (ˆx′ˆz′) region is by [27] shown

From Eqs. (3.12), (3.13), (3.17) and (3.18) is seen that Evv and Ehh represents the
desired co-polar terms, while the cross terms Ehv and Evh are the unwanted side-eﬀect
of θ0 ̸= 0. The amplitude of the cross terms are seen to be inversely proportional to
the focal length to diameter ratio, F/D, through the variable τ, and thus in agreement
with the analysis of the surface currents in the previous subsection.
The co-polar term consists of both an in-phase and a quadrature term, where the
later is of the same magnitude and location as the cross-polar term. That a co-polar
quadrature component exists is not important, since it does not eﬀect the polarization
purity of the antenna. The cross-polar term for both polarizations of the incident ﬁeld
has the exact same shape but with opposite sign.
Although the above analysis is based strictly on a plane wave incident on the
reﬂector at boresight, the location and phase of the cross-polar component has shown
to hint a far more eﬀective solution than that of increasing the F/D ratio.

Solution Proposal
Applying the reciprocity theorem, in order to transmit a wave similar to the plane
wave in the boresight direction, the horn must generate ﬁelds showing the same
approximate ﬁeld distribution as that shown above. A conventional corrugated horn
provides only a match to the in-phase component of the co-polar term, and thus is
expected to radiate something diﬀerent than that of the co-polarized plane wave [3].
It has been known since the 1970s that the concept of a matched feed may sig-
niﬁcantly reduces the cross-polar radiation. Without going into details, the idea is
to generate a conjugate match to the focal region electric ﬁeld above by introducing
higher order modes in the horn. The conjugation of the ﬁeld corresponds to reversing
the direction of propagation.
Depending on the type of feed, diﬀerent modes are needed, with the combined
ﬁeld having a varying degree of similarity to that of the desired focal region ﬁeld.
Furthermore, the excited mode or modes most be generated at a correct amplitude
and with a proper phase relationship and position relative to that of the primary
mode. An inherent problem of using multiple modes with diﬀerent phase velocities
is bandwidth, with the cancellation of the cross-polarization in its nature being very
phase sensitive [29].
Experimental veriﬁcations of the matched-feed concept are numerous, citing here
only a few: [3,8] obtains an additional suppression of the cross-polar level of at least
10 dB, [5,6] theoretically obtain a 16 dB additional suppression, [7] demonstrates a
well match mode generator, and [29] obtains a 20 dB reduction with a 10 dB, 10%
bandwidth using a measured horn and simulated secondary pattern. For all the ex-
==PAGE:56==
36
CHAPTER 3. THE SOURCE OF CROSS POLARIZATION
Figure 3.9.
The Cobham SATCOM EXPLORER 8100 Auto-Acquire Drive-Away
antenna. The antenna utilizes the oﬀset reﬂector antenna. Source: [4]
amples given above, the horn is stationary and ﬁxed in orientation with respect to the
reﬂector, a condition which is not possible for a satellite communication application
utilizing linear polarization unless other measures are taken2.
Providing the conjugate match at the feed horn aperture may not be the only
way to increase the polarization purity.
In this work, it is proposed to insert a
2D screen between the feed and the oﬀset parabolic reﬂector, which is supposed to
generate the needed cross-polar quadrature component. The problem is investigated
using two orthogonal polarizations that, if solved, will constitute a solution for an
arbitrary polarization. The surface will be ﬁxed relative to the reﬂector enabling an
axisymmetric horn to be rotated arbitrarily. This feature is of interest in a satellite
communication sitting as explained in the next section.
3.3
Product Speciﬁcations and Requirements
To limit the scope of the investigation, the project will focus on a particular product
from Cobham SATCOM: The EXPLORER 8100 Auto-Acquire Drive-Away antenna
for mounting on the roof of a car [4]. The antenna is shown on Fig. 3.9 with physical
dimensions and selected speciﬁcations shown in Table 3.1.
The EXPLORER 8100
will serve as the medium to quantize the problem and solution, thus we will use the
oﬀset reﬂector geometry speciﬁed by Table 3.1 in all simulations, a long with the
feed pattern of the horn.
The feed itself is an axial corrugated horn followed by
an orthogonal mode transducer to separate the signals into the linear receive and

3.4
Summary
This chapter has investigated the source of cross-polarization in the far-ﬁeld of an
oﬀset reﬂector antenna. Coordinate systems to describe the ﬁelds has been deﬁned
and will be used throughout the remainder of this work. The existence of a cross-polar
component has been demonstrated in the secondary radiation pattern, even with a
perfect co-polarized Huygens’ source as feed. The primary source is identiﬁed, being
the curvature the paraboloid and the missing symmetry of the oﬀset conﬁguration.
==PAGE:59==
3.4. SUMMARY
39
One way of mitigating the problem is by increasing the F/D ratio.
An analysis by [27] is repeated, showing analytical formulas for the electric ﬁeld in
the focal plane region when the reﬂector is illuminated by a plane wave at boresight.
The analysis reveals a cross-polarized component in quadrature with the co-polar
component. Historically, the concept of matched feed has been applied with success,
wherein a conjugate match to the focal plane ﬁelds is achieved using higher order
modes in the feed horn.
However, there is no reason why this match cannot be
achieve at a arbitrary distance in front of the horn, and using other methods than
higher order waveguide modes.
Due to time limitations, this work is focused on reducing the level of cross polar
radiation on a particular product from Cobham SATCOM: The EXPLORER 8100,
1 m oﬀset reﬂector antenna. The EXPLORER 8100 has a weather protecting screen
located 5 mm in front of the feed horn. The purpose of this work is to investigate and
design a 2D screen, that when substituted with the weather protection screen could
solve the cross-polarization issue in the transmitting band of the antenna.
==PAGE:60==
==PAGE:61==
4
Physical Optics Program
In order to investigate the phenomena in question, a MATLAB script based on physi-
cal optics and the theory outlined in Section 2.2 is developed. The script is printed in
Appendix D.2 along with all other relevant MATLAB scripts. Commercial simulation
tools like GRASP [21] are available, but are not versatile enough to act as the main
tool of the project. GRASP is however used as a veriﬁcation tool in Section 4.4.
4.1
Outline
The oﬀset reﬂector antenna in this project consist of a reﬂector and a feed. The
reﬂector is a subset of a paraboloid with the geometry deﬁned in Section 2.1 using
the parameters in Table 3.1 and is assumed a PEC. The feed pattern is extracted
from CHAMP and GRASP as a truncated plane of equivalent current sources and
excites a surface current on the reﬂector which is approximated by physical optics.
The primary coordinate system will be that of Cartesian (x,y,z) system deﬁned
in Section 3.1 and Fig. 3.1, where all vector calculus are performed. The choice of
coordinate system dictates the meshing of the discretization as will be evident in what
follows.
The script roughly performing the following tasks:
1. Deﬁne the meshing and normal vector of the reﬂector by calculating selected
coordinates of the reﬂector surface.
2. Import E and H-ﬁeld grids of the horn excitation from GRASP in the plane of
the weather screen, denormalize and convert to equivalent electric and magnetic
surface currents. The horn excitation is found using the near-ﬁeld radiation
equations in Section 2.2.
3. Calculate the magnetic ﬁeld at all data points on the reﬂector from bullet 1,
looping through all data points of the excitation grid of bullet 2.
4. Apply physical optics approximation.
5. Calculate the far-ﬁeld electric ﬁeld at all coordinates in the desired far-ﬁeld
grid, looping through all data points on the reﬂector surface using the radia-
tion equations. As this project is only concerned with the radiation close to
boresight, the direct contribution from the feed is neglected
6. Convert to co- and cross-polar directivity.
Any superimposed current from the 2D screen located at the weather screen may
easily by added to the current grid of bullet 2. The remainder of this chapter will
clarify how the program is coded, the discretization of relevant equations and the
veriﬁcation process.
41
==PAGE:62==
42
CHAPTER 4. PHYSICAL OPTICS PROGRAM

ﬁnd the reﬂector surface area A. The circle illustrates the true circle of the projected
aperture. The meshing is shown with N = 5, of which only the black dots are actually
part of the reﬂector. When N is large, a proper representation of a circle is achieved.

discrete quantities must be used.

system:

where f0 is the zeroth order Taylor expansion of the integrand in Eq. (4.4) expanded
at (y, z).
Far-ﬁeld Radiation.
The magnetic ﬁeld radiated by the feed is evaluated at the
center coordinate of each small square coordinate, (x, y, z), and assumed uniform
over the entire square. The surface integration of the reﬂector current of Eq. (2.50)
yielding the radiation pattern is converted into two dimensional summation over all
the small squares constituting the reﬂector:
E(θ, φ) = jωµ

where A and J are the surface area and current of each small square corresponding
to the indexes combination (ny, nz), the distance vector R is set to unit length, and
phase correcting constants from Green’s function is omitted. The later two changes
has no implication when calculating the ﬁnal directivity following Eq. (2.10).
In practice, the coordinates of each square, (x,y,z), are calculated and stored
in three 2D matrices, after which all calculations are carried out running through
the indices of ny and nz.
Translating A(y, z) into A(ny, nz) is thus trivial when
implemented.

Additional Comments.
A rule of thump when discretization a continuous dis-
tributed variable is to have at least 10 points per wavelength. Interpreting the wave-
length as that of the design frequency grossly overestimate the number of points
needed. Given the 1 m reﬂector, the number of data points would arrive at 471×471 ≈
220 000. As seen in Fig. 3.4, the data points used are far less than this. The wave-
length should be interpreted as that of the change over the aperture but in any case
chosen as a value large enough to achieve convergences in the far-ﬁeld response.
As is evident from the above, no higher order integration procedures are used to
keep the implementation as simple as possible. This is the case for both summing
over the reﬂector and the source region of the next section. Had the purpose of the
project been to perform the calculations as fast as possible, implementing for instance
the trapezoidal rule could reduce the number of data points needed.
4.3
Source Modeling
The feed horn used by the EXPLORER 8100 is an axial corrugated horn designed
in CHAMP. It is desired to represent the horn as a grid of equivalent current using
the surface equivalence theorem of Section 2.2. With reference to Fig. 4.2, imagine a
cylindrical box encapsulating the horn, assuming a zero equivalent current at all sides
but the one facing the reﬂector. The axis of the cylinder coincide with the rotational
axis of the horn, with the none zero ﬂat end parallel to the horn aperture. Denoting
==PAGE:65==
4.3. SOURCE MODELING
45
the ﬁeld inside the cylinder as (E, H) the currents radiating approximately the same
ﬁelds are given by
Js = ˆy′ × H,
(4.10)
Ms = −ˆy′ × E.
(4.11)
The none zero side is again chosen as a square grid and deﬁned in the (x′, y′, z′)
coordinate system analog to that of the reﬂector projected aperture in Fig. 4.1. The
grid size is denoted Nsource × Nsource. The distance from the horn aperture to the
source grid is chosen equal to the distance between the horn and the protecting
weather screen, 5 mm.
Each small square in the grid is designated by a center coordinate given by
(x′, 40 mm, z′) with an area of As depending on the number of points exported from
GRASP (Nsource) and the physical size of cylinder enclosing the horn. Each square
is again evaluated whether it is within the radius of the cylinder following the lines
of Eq. (4.3):
Dsource
2
≥
p
x′2 + z′2,
(4.12)
where Dsource is the diameter of the enclosing cylinder.
The resolution of the grid is chosen as a trade-oﬀbetween computation time and
accuracy.
The meshing is evident on Fig. 4.3, where the plotted square directly
corresponds to the small squares mentioned above. The ﬁeld grid is extracted for
one polarization only and, inspired by the real feed, the orthogonal polarization is
achieved by rotation the grid 90°.
Extracting the 2D Grid.
The horn used for the EXPLORER 8100 is designed
in CHAMP. Unfortunatly, extracting the desired grid directly from CHAMP is not
possible and a very cumbersome approach is needed. A spherical wave expansion
(SWE) is exported and imported into GRASP, from where it is converted into a
plane wave expansion (PWE), which is then used to evaluate the ﬁeld at the desired
distance.
Without going into detail, a SWE is an expansion into orthogonal spherical modes
of diﬀerent weights. The SWE may be generated when the ﬁeld is known on a sphere
of constant radius and accurately represents the ﬁeld outside a sphere containing
the source. GRASP denotes the smallest possible sphere, with the center used for
the expansion, containing the source as the minimum sphere. Within the minimum
sphere, GRASP cannot compute the radiated ﬁelds [19]. Unfortunately, the weather
screen is located well inside the minimum sphere, but using the PWE expansion it is
possible to calculate the desired ﬁeld grid.
A PWE is an expansion into plane waves of diﬀerent weightings and directions.
The expansion is analog to that of the SWE, but in this case based on the knowing
the ﬁeld in a grid rather than a sphere. GRASP calculates the PWE based on the
far-ﬁeld radiation from the SWE source, and may be propagated backwards towards
the original source to any given distance. Since the PWE is based on the far-ﬁeld
==PAGE:66==
46
CHAPTER 4. PHYSICAL OPTICS PROGRAM
radiation, it cannot contain any information about the near-ﬁeld evanescent waves.
Whether or not the obtained ﬁeld grids are equivalent to that of the horn is an open
question, but veriﬁed through later simulations to be of suﬃcient accuracy.
The desired 2D grids of tangential electric and magnetic ﬁelds are exported and
read into MATLAB using ReadGRASP_grdﬁle.m in Appendix D.7.
Normalized Units and Power.
GRASP’s internal engine utilizes root mean square
values measured in the normalized unit of W
1
2 1. The relation between the ﬁelds in
standard SI units and the units in GRASP is given by
ESI = k
p
2η EGRASP,
(4.13)
HSI =
s
2k
η HGRASP,
(4.14)
where ESI and HSI are the electric and magnetic ﬁelds measured in SI units and
peak amplitude values, respectively, and EGRASP and HGRASP are the corresponding
quantities in GRASP. Furthermore, sources may be normalized to a total power of
4π W, which is used in this project. A consequence of the unit and power normal-
ization is, that the expression for the directivity reduces to that of the electric ﬁeld.
Plotting one is thus equivalent to plotting the other [19].
No eﬀord is done in this project to normalize the source to any other power level
than 4π W. Thus whenever an absolute number is presented, this value is normalized
to a horn radiating 4π W. In comparison, the power stage of the EXPLORER 8100
is rated at 8 or 20 W depending on the customer. When GRASP data is loaded into
the MATLAB script, the units are reverted to SI units.
Radiation.
The radiated H-ﬁeld by the source at the reﬂector is calculated us-
ing the near-ﬁeld radiation equation of both a magnetic and electric current. The
equations are discretized in the same way as described for the far-ﬁeld radiation.
From a computational point of view, calculating the ﬁeld using the near-ﬁeld
formulas and based on both the electric and magnetic current is a signiﬁcant mistake.
The distance between the reﬂector and horn aperture is suﬃciently large to use the
simpliﬁed far-ﬁeld equations. Furthermore, since we are only interested in the ﬁeld
in parts of one half plane, replacing the other with a PEC or PMC and eliminating
one current would further reduce the number of computations.

Source radiation pattern
The source is modeled using magnetic and electric ﬁeld grids converted to equivalent
surface currents in Eqs. (4.10) and (4.11) and shown in Fig. 4.3. The shape of the
currents and ﬁelds are as expected from a circular horn with the mode TE11 bending
outwards for x′ = z′ = ±2 mm, thus creating the cross-polar current in Fig. 4.3(b).
The radiated ﬁeld is expected to closely represent that of the Huygens’, as it is tapered
equally in all directions.
To verify a correct implementation, the far-ﬁeld response is calculated and com-
pared to that of the original SWE source in Fig. 4.4(a). A very good correspondence
is seen for an angle far greater than that of the cone deﬁning the oﬀset reﬂector.

essentially zero outside the chosen diameter.
As stated earlier, it is not possible to verify the near ﬁeld response directly, since
the actual near-ﬁeld from the horn is not readily available using the SWE represen-
tation.
Total Radiated Power
GRASP can normalize sources to 4π W radiated power, which is what is used in this
project. However, the total radiated power is computed in order to verify correct
implementation of normalization along with unit conversions.
==PAGE:69==
4.4. VERIFICATION
49
The validation is done in two ways: Summing Poynting vector over a closed sphere
and over the truncated 2D grid assuming plane wave behavior propagating in the
normal direction. The former is the theoretically correct method but requires a much
greater number of computations [11,19]. Both are performed in calculate_Prad.m of
Appendix D.3 and arrive at 3.91π W radiated power.
It is concluded that about 2.2 % of the power from the horn is radiated in the
backwards direction and, most important, that the implementation and interpretation
of GRASP data is correct.
In order to arrive at the correct directivity, the radiated power is set to the original
4π W as the wasted 2.2 % is indeed radiated by the source but not accounted for in
the script.
Secondary Radiation Pattern
Reﬂector Surface.
The reﬂector surface, surface normal and surface area calcula-
tion are only validated by plotting the surface and normal vector. Both has already
been plotted in Fig. 3.5, where the result is as expected.
Hertzian Dipoles.
The MATLAB program is tested against that of GRASP us-
ing hertzian dipoles.
A number of tests has been completed using both electric
and magnetic dipoles at diﬀerent locations, most of which are not shown in the re-
port. The veriﬁcation is computed using the main MATLAB program and the script
TestScriptHertzianDipole.m of Appendix D.3.
Figure 4.5 compares the directivity using an electric dipole source located at
(x′, y′, z′) = (30 mm, 5 mm, 0), with a very ﬁne meshing of the reﬂector, N = 401. The
meshing of the reﬂector is deﬁned through the variable N mentioned in Eq. (4.2), with
the number of data point on the reﬂector totaling at N 2.
Figure 4.5(f) displays the error as given by Eq. (4.15). It is seen, that the MAT-
LAB script is very accurate and even able to follow GRASP into the correct depth
of zeroes. The error is especially low in the proximity of the main lobe. A directiv-
ity plot using a magnetic dipole is shown in Fig. C.1 in Appendix C using a more
appropriate meshing of the reﬂector: N = 101.
It is desired to have a low value of N, as N is inversely proportional to the
computation time. A fast computation time is essential for later use in optimization
algorithms.
Figs. C.2 and C.3 displays two more veriﬁcation plots using dipoles
located at the the focal point and signiﬁcantly displaced from the focal point. Both
are computer using N = 71 and shows a good correspondence with GRASP. Especially
the centered dipole has no error for θ = ±2°. It is thus concluded, that using N = 101
provides a more than suﬃcient convergences of the far-ﬁeld response.
N = 101
corresponds to 2.1 data points pr. free space wavelength at 14.125 GHz.

4.5
Summary
A MATLAB script has been developed which correctly calculates the secondary radi-
ation pattern of the oﬀset reﬂector antenna within 5° to boresight. The script is based
on physical optics and a surface equivalence representation of the feed horn, where
the direct contribution from the feed is neglected. The discretization of relevant equa-
tions describing the reﬂector and ﬁeld quantities are outlined, and the overall ﬂow of
the MATLAB program is presented.
The script is validated extensively against an identical project in GRASP using
both the original SWE source and Hertzian dipoles. The former has validated the
source representation, and the later the reﬂector geometry and far-ﬁeld calculations.
The combined script is validated comparing the secondary radiation pattern of the
equivalent sources to that of the equivalent system build in GRASP. The result is
concluded convergent for a square source grid of with 101 × 101 data points and a
square reﬂector grid with 101 × 101 data points.

==PAGE:73==
5
Current and Particle Design
As shortly outlined in Section 3.2, this project intends to solve the cross-polar ra-
diation problem by introducing some sort of screen between the feed horn and the
reﬂector. The screen is located 5 mm in front of the horn aperture and 4 cm away from
the focal point. Figure 5.1 displays the basic idea perused in this project, showing
the location of the screen in scale.
A solution is investigated using the superposition principle, where when solved for
two orthogonal polarizations of the feed, the problem is solved for an arbitrary horn
polarization. This is possible since all materials and governing equations are linear.
The success of the matched feed outlined in Section 3.2 has shown, that a conju-
gate match to the focal plane ﬁeld may substantially reduce the radiated cross-polar
component at angles diﬀerent from boresight. By assuming a plane wave behavior,
both the E and H focal plane ﬁelds are available, and the needed current in the
plane of the screen may be calculated from the boundary conditions of Eqs. (2.54)
and (2.55). The ﬁeld generated by the horn are already calculated and shown in
Fig. 4.3. The boundary currents are thus given by the diﬀerence between the focal
plane ﬁelds and those radiated by the horn. Unfortunately, the discrepancy of the
ﬁelds are of such a magnitude, that any information gained from analyzing the focal
plane ﬁeld are completely buried and simply not evident on the boundary currents.
There may be several reasons for this. The horn is not optimized to resemble a plane
wave at boresight, but rather to achieve the largest possible directivity taking spillover
and other eﬀects into account. Furthermore, the ﬁelds used for the horn was those
subtracted 4 cm ≈2λ from the focal plane, leaving a possibly very large discrepancy.
In any case, as will be parent later, the problem is not to identify the needed
current but to generate them. The return of investment on time deriving an expression
for the focal plane ﬁelds at y′ = 4 cm or vice versa is considered low.
Possibly
derived currents would not be suggestive to a solution, nor is it possible to generate
a equivalent to the magnetic current in a strictly two dimensional structure.
Providing a Partical Match
By doing a qualitative assessment between the focal
plane and the horn ﬁelds suﬃcient information is obtained. The two ﬁelds are shown
in Figs. 3.7 and 4.3 respectively. The co-polar component in Fig. 3.7(a) resembles
that of Fig. 4.3(d) while the cross-polar component in Fig. 3.7(b) as no resemblance
to Fig. 4.3(c) at all. The cross-polar component of the source or horn is essentially
zero at the desired local of (x′, z′) = (0, ±14)mm, why a solution reduces to creating
a conjugate match to the major feature of the focal plane cross polar ﬁeld. This
conclusion is essentially the same as that leading to the matched feed concept, but in
this case with a diﬀerent solution in mind.
It is desired to investigate the actual far-ﬁeld response from providing a partial
match to the cross-polar focal plane ﬁeld. In this case it is more intuitive to think
53
==PAGE:74==
54
CHAPTER 5. CURRENT AND PARTICLE DESIGN
Figure 5.1. Illustration of the basic idea of the proposed solution in correct size ratio.
The brown screen is the oﬀset reﬂector, the green cylinder is the feed, and the plane
of the weather screen is marked with the blue square grid measuring 16 × 16 cm.
The blue grid also indicates the plane and size from which the source ﬁeld veriﬁed in
Section 4.3 originates. The graphic is obtained from GRASP [21] and only the origin
of the two coordinate system displays correspond to that of the normal and oﬀset
used in this work.
of the superimposed current as one whose far-ﬁeld response cancels the unwanted
cross-polar radiation. One such simple implementation would be to match only the
peak of Fig. 3.7(b) by superimposing electrical current. Denoting the current at the
screen as Jscreen
Jscreen(x′, y′, z′) = δ(y′ −y′
screen)Jxz(x′, z′)
(5.1)
where δ is the Dirac’s delta function and y′
screen = 4 cm is the coordinate of the
weather protection screen. The current in the plane of the screen is thus given by
Jxz(x′, z′) in two coordinates only.
5.1
Superimposed Hertzian Dipoles
The simplest possible match one can imagine is two Hertzian dipoles each representing
a peak in Fig. 3.7(b). Figure 5.2 shows the dipoles located symmetrically around the
symmetry plane near the focal point. The secondary radiation of the dipoles are to
be equal in amplitude and out of phase to the cross-polar radiation generated by the
reﬂector, in order for a superposition of the two to cancel. From this fact alone, it
immediately follows that the dipole moments are to be oriented orthogonal to the
primary polarization of the source, exactly as predicted by the the focal plane ﬁelds.
==PAGE:75==
5.1. SUPERIMPOSED HERTZIAN DIPOLES
55
Figure 5.2. Two superimposed Hertzian dipoles oriented along the ˆx′-axis in front of
the oﬀset reﬂector antenna. The equivalent currents of the horn excitation are not
shown. For the oﬀset coordinate system (closest to the dipoles), the red is ˆx′, green
is −ˆz and blue corresponds to ˆy′.
From the orientation of the dipoles in Fig. 5.2, it is evident that feed is horizontally
polarized.
It is implicitly assumed that the superimposed dipoles may be designed indepen-
dently of the inﬂuence of the horn. I.e. that a structure may be designed without
mutual coupling to the horn. The validity of this assumption is addressed in Sec-
tion 6.3.

given a vertical or horizontal polarized feed denoted by ˆafeed

From the focal plane analysis, a good initial guess would be I1 = −I2, and one
could manually adjust the amplitude and absolute phase to cancel in the far-ﬁeld
based on the phase at boresight2. The location of the dipoles would be that of the

This indeed yields an improvement but is here replaced by optimization routines,
since they result in similar results. Whether the obtained solution is the best possi-
ble is an open question. Especially the location, phase relationship and the number
of dipoles are worth investigation, why the remainder of this section will consist of
diﬀerent MATLAB optimization algorithms to determine just this. The setup is opti-
mized with position, phase and amplitude of both as free variables while maintaining
the mirror position.
Optimization on Two Independent Dipoles.
In the section, the phase relation-
ship of the two dipoles are tested. Since the reﬂector conﬁguration is symmetric, the
location of the dipoles are maintained in the mirror conﬁgaration but allowed to be
moved in on both axis (x′
1 = x′
2 and z′
1 = −z′
2).
1In the discretization of Eqs. (5.2) and (5.3), the two Dirac’s delta functions simple corresponds

The optimization is performed with respect to the comments in Section 2.3, called
in PO_optimization.m, and using PO_optimization_func_2dipoles_free.m as the
objective function. All MATLAB ﬁles used in this chapter is found in Appendix D.4.
Note that the position of the dipoles are locked to the discrete values of the source
grid with a resolution of 1.6 mm. The grid size of the reﬂector grid is N = 71 and
number of points in the far-ﬁeld is Nfarﬁeld = 100.
The optimization function penalizes the peak cross-polar radiation in an angle
of θ = ±2° to boresight in ﬁve φ-cuts uniformly distributed between zero and 90°.
It is chosen to minimize the peak rather than the ratio of co- and cross-polarized
directivity in order to arrive at a more general and robust solution (as opposed to
the co/cross-polar ratio 1 dB down from the main lobe as otherwise required in by
Eutelsat in Section 3.3).
Figure 5.4 displays the result compared to the unaltered radiation pattern of the
oﬀset reﬂector antenna for a vertical polarized source. A similar result is obtained
using a horizontal polarized feed. The co-polar directivity is not shown as there is
no visible diﬀerence. This is expected, since co-polar directivity and total power of
the dipoles are much smaller than the original feed. Furthermore, the conversion
from cross-polar radiating of the dipoles to co-polar incurs a signiﬁcant penalty or
reduction.

feed, where Dcx,max is the maximum of cross-polarized directivity. The relative phase

It is noted, that the radiated power from the dipole is not included in the total
radiated power of 4π W used to calculate the directivity. In all fairness, the directivity

It is seen that a signiﬁcant improvement in obtained, with the peak cross polariza-
tion down to 5.8 dB and 5.1 dB from 17.2 dB for a ˆv and ˆh polarized feed respectively.
The optimization was free to choose the phase, but the expected phase diﬀerence close
to 180° was nevertheless identiﬁed.
The amplitude of the two dipoles are not equal, nor are they 180° out of phase.
Inspecting Fig. 5.4 onces more, it is seen that the φ = 45° is surprisingly symmetric, a
symmetry that does not exist in geometry of oﬀset reﬂector. The optimization seem
to have chosen the currents to obtain the mentioned symmetry. Had the cut been
made for φ = −45°, the diﬀerence in amplitude and phase wold have been reversed.
The assumption of equal amplitude is thus maintained as necessary to obtain the best
result for all φ cuts.
The location of the dipoles are seen close to the peaks of the cross-polar focal plane
ﬁelds being (x′, z′) = (0, 14)mm. The location closer to (x′, z′) = (0, 0) is opposite of
what would be expected, since the plane of the screen is closer to the reﬂector. A ray
interpretation would expect the rays to diverge when propagating forward towards
the reﬂector. The identiﬁed location of z′ = 9.6 mm might very well be a numerically
better solution for the θ = ±2° requirement on Dcx.
A noticeable diﬀerence in the x′ coordinate between the two primary polarization
are observed with no obvious explanation. The diﬀerence is however small compared
to any meaningful physically realizable structure at this frequency.
The achieved reductions of Table 5.1 are by no means the lower limit, since there
is no guaranty that the global optimizer has found it way to the very bottom of the
global minimum. After multiple runs, the author is conﬁdent that the location is
indeed the global maximum.
