Better Result Closer to the Focal Point.
The implication of moving the im-
pressed current closer to the focal plane has shortly been investigated. The results
are summarized in Table 5.2. In this case only the amplitude and phase diﬀerence
are shown, since the absolute phase is somewhat arbitrary4.
It is clear that y′ coordinate of the dipoles has a signiﬁcant inﬂuence on the
resulting reduction and that the cross-polar directivity obtained in Table 5.2 are
better than those in Table 5.1 by about 4 dB. The location of the dipoles and the
relative phase is as expected.
It is concluded that an optimal solution should be closer to the focal plane. This
makes intuitive sense that currents should be located near the focal point, since the
rays from the reﬂector collimates close to the focal point and not at the weather
screen. When the feed of an ordinary paraboloid reﬂector antenna is moved from the
focal point, the radiation pattern becomes asymmetric and the main beam is squinted
away from boresight5. The eﬀect is unwanted as the intend is to cancel a symmetric
pattern close to boresight.
The dependence on y′ will not be pursued further and y′ = 40 mm in the remained
of this work unless otherwise stated.
Phase and Amplitude of Dipole Pair Locked.
It was concluded above, that
the amplitudes of the two dipoles should be equal. For a real implementation using
some particle or particles located in the weather-screen plane, the following key points
are important to note:
1. The current on the particles has to be driven by the ﬁeld of the feed. With the
cross-polar component being almost zero, the co-polar ﬁeld has to generate the
needed current.
2. The co-polar E-ﬁeld is rotational symmetric why the local phase and amplitude
seen by the two symmetrically located dipoles are identical.
4The phase is deﬁned relative to the center of the spherical wave at y′ = 40 mm, but since
the dipoles are not located in this plane, one would have to account for the phase gained during
propagation.
5Examples of the squinting is visible on all but the center located Hertzian dipole veriﬁcation
plots, see Section 4.4 and Appendix C.

physical implementation will inevitable smooth the current over a larger area.

Parameter Study
A ﬁeld component cancellation is naturally very sensitive to the amplitude and phase
of the compensating signal. Fig. 5.8 displays a parameter study varying the amplitude
and phase of the symmetrically mirrored dipole pair conﬁguration. The locations are
chosen equal to those identiﬁed as optimal in Table 5.3.
It is seen, that the resulting cross-polar level is very sensitive to the amplitude,
and somewhat insensitive to the phase. A signiﬁcant diﬀerence is present comparing
the two feed polarizations. The solution space for a horizontal polarized feed is sig-
niﬁcantly larger and with a smaller amplitude. A small magnitude is highly desirable
as this translates into a smaller coupling to a physical structure and thus easier to
realize in practice.
Figure 5.9 repeats the plot of Fig. 5.8, but with the same locations for both
polarization of the feed. The amplitude is now more comparable, and the needed
phase shift is signiﬁcantly smaller.
The later should not be a surprise, since the
position is moved away from the leading phase center of the feed. The phase shift
may thus be smaller, while at the same time possess an unchanged relative phase to
the center of the grid. The target area is about the same, and with the same possible
improvement of the crosspolar directivity. It is concluded, that the exact position in
the weather screen plane is not the most important parameter, rather how the phase,
amplitude and location are optimized together.

5.2
A rotation of the incident ﬁeld
With the idea of superimposed currents veriﬁed to work, it is desirable to look further
into orientation of the currents relative to the incident ﬁeld. Figure 5.10 shows the
incident ﬁeld and current vector for the two orthogonal polarizations. Figure is shown
in scale relative to the horn aperture with the positions in Table 5.1.
It turns out that the needed currents are polarization rotated 90° degrees and
delayed about 80° in time.
The rotation is counter-clockwise for z′ positive and
==PAGE:85==
5.3. PHYSICAL REALIZATION
65
clockwise for z′ negative for both polarization of the incident ﬁeld.
This has the
important consequence, that an implementation for z′ negative is that of z′ positive
mirrored in the symmetry plane. Furthermore, given a solution for one polarization,
the solution for the second is an identical particle rotated 90°.
Chirality.
Chirality was ﬁrst mention by Lord Kelvin and may be deﬁned using a
quote:
“I call any geometrical ﬁgure, or group of points, chiral, and say it has
chirality, if its image in a plane mirror, ideally realized, cannot be brought
to coincide with itself.” — Lord Kelvin, 1904 [31].
The term is usually applied in three dimensions, since any 2D structure may be
brought back to the original state when rotated out of its residing plane. When this
action is prohibited, a 2D structure may indeed be chiral [31]. The operation with
opposite rotation directions require the solution to be chiral: A none chiral particle
would look identical before and after mirroring, with the result that any rotation
must be in the same direction. Mirroring corresponds to seeing the structure from
the backside.
Intuitively, the easiest way of conjuring a possible solution is to design a structure
or particle for one polarization only while assuming it is invisible to second. To obtain
the full solution, rotate the original particle 90° and superimpose it on the original.
This novel approach thus requires the solution to be 4-fold rotational symmetric.
Nevertheless, without stating any proof, this requirement is not general (a further
discussion of this in Section 7.1) [31].
To summarize, a practical solution must be chiral and are further assumed to have
a 4-fold symmetry.
5.3
Physical Realization
It is proposed to generate the needed currents using a dipole like scatter, and do so
by considering one polarization at a time. The dipole is chosen because of its simple
structure and due to the fact that a short dipole will introduce a phase shift between
the incident ﬁeld and the generated current.
The Feasibility of the Solution.
Whether there is enough energy in the incident
ﬁeld to be collected and re-radiated is an open question. By using dipoles it is possible
to calculate the available power of the incident ﬁeld of a plane wave and thus evaluate
whether there is enough energy in the ﬁeld to generate the needed dipole moment.
The available power of a plane wave is a function of directivity for a lossless
antenna. According to [11, Ch. 4], the directivity of a Hertzian dipole is 1.5 dB and
that of a short dipole about 1.64 dB depending on the length. The incident ﬁeld 8 mm
from the center has Eco ≈3 kV m−1, while the cross-polar ﬁeld exhibits four distinct
peaks at (x′, y′) = (±1, ±1) mm with Ecx ≈150 V m−1. Using Eqs. (2.15) and (2.16),
==PAGE:86==
66
CHAPTER 5. CURRENT AND PARTICLE DESIGN
the ﬁelds corresponds to an available power of 6.9 W and 17 mW for Eco and Ecx
respectively when D = 1.5 dB is used. All absolute quantities are normalized to a
total radiated power of 4π = 12.6 W.
The required dipoles moment estimated earlier in this chapter as I ≈700 µA m,
correspond to a power of 430 mW according to
PHertzianDipole = η π
3

I
λ

2
(5.11)
given by [11] for the power of a Hertzian dipole. In comparing with the available
power it is concluded, that the co-polar ﬁeld has suﬃcient energy by a factor of 16
and that the cross-polar ﬁeld may be ignored. A solution has to convert the co-polar
ﬁeld from the feed into a cross-polar oriented current as describer in Section 5.2.
Achieving the 90° Phase Shift.
The equivalent circuit of a scatterer is that of the
same structure used as an antenna loaded by a short. If the dipole is chosen capac-
itative, a phase shift approaching 90° is possible. Shorting the equivalent Thévenin
equivalent and isolating the current we get

required to use a dipole shorter than about 7.6 mm.
Unfortunately, the length is inverse proportional to the amount of power mis-
match. The maximum available powers calculated earlier apply for an impedance
matched antenna, which in this case translates into how close the scatterer is to res-
onance. The larger the phase shift, the smaller is the excited current on the element
and the factor of 16 may quickly be lost. No attempt has been made to quantify the
miss match for two reasons: The Thévenin equivalent is only a valid representation
of a circuit outside of the equivalent itself, and that an analytical expression for the
impedance does not exist for a modiﬁed dipole.
Dipole Moment Generated by a Plane Wave.
By using a EFIE-MoM engine
it is possible to quantify the excited current. The dipole is chosen as ﬂat and made of
PEC. Unfortunately neither GRASP nor WIPL-D has a functionality to export the
current for processing in MATLAB. WIPL-D happens to store the currents in the local
coordinate system to each plate in a ﬁle, and a MATLAB script, ReadWIPL_ﬁles.m,
is developed to convert the current into the coordinate system framework used in
this work. The script is included in Appendix D.7 with a technical description in
Appendix B. To convert the surface currents into an approximate dipole moment
to compare with the Hertzian dipoles of the previous section, the script sums the
contribution over the entire structure.
Performing the simulation with a ﬂat dipole 6.5 mm in length and 0.2 mm in
width generate a dipole moment of approximately 200 µA m when experiencing no
polarization mismatch. The result is about one third of the desired dipole moment
and suggests that multiple dipoles are needed, but that a solution might be possible.
Two Tilted Dipoles Cancel.
Initially, a solution using two tilted dipoles was
investigated. Two tilted dipoles are not chiral and thus cannot possibly work, but is
included to demonstrate the problem of canceling currents.

One isolated dipole if tilted relative to the incoming ﬁeld, would generate a cross-
polar current. Using two such tilted dipoles perpendicular to each could possibly be
the solution, as they isolated performs the desired operation for each of the two inci-
dent polarizations. Fig. 5.12 displays the conﬁguration generating a counter rotated
current.
Given a plane wave with the electric ﬁeld vector Ex polarized along ˆx in Fig. 5.12(a),
the shown particle experiences a ﬁeld along ˆa of Ea which excites a current Ia along
the ﬂat dipole given by

Increase The Eﬀectively By Adding A Bend.
When a bend is added to the
conﬁguration of Fig. 5.12, the resulting particle is chiral. A such bend is shown in
Fig. 5.13(a) for the ˆv-particle particle.
The bend further increases the amount of current running in the cross-polar di-
rection. When the current on the dipole is assumed sinusoidal, the majority of the
current is concentrated in the middle. By turning only the center part entirely in the
cross-polar direction, the sin(α) expression of the tilted dipoles is avoided.
The phase of the current seems to be proportional to the total length, while the
coupling to the driving electric ﬁeld is only proportional to the length along the E-
ﬁeld. Thus a trade-oﬀexist which is not attempted quantiﬁed. The exact dimensions
of the particle will determined by a optimization procedure.
The bend dipole conﬁguration also suﬀers from a canceling of current but to a
lesser degree than the tilted dipole. This is shown in Fig. 5.13(b). The green and red
arrows correspond to the currents generated by a vertical and horizontal polarized
feed respectively. For a counter-clockwise rotation it is seen that the particle labeled
Px′ performs the desire operation for a ˆx′-polarized source (ˆv), and likewise for Pz′
and a ˆz′-polarized source. Unfortunately, when an ˆx′-polarized is incident on Pz′, the
current in the outer arms of the particle are opposite to the desired current. The net
current generated in ˆz′ is thus the diﬀerence between the two contributions.
==PAGE:90==
70
CHAPTER 5. CURRENT AND PARTICLE DESIGN
Figure 5.14.
Particle in WIPL-D using a ﬂat PEC. The red arrow indicates the
orientation of electric ﬁeld from a plane wave incident on the particle, and is seen to
line up with the longest dimention of the bend dipole. The excited current of interest
in this particular case is that in the ˆz′ direction and labeled IP′x.
The undesired
current IP′x is obtained by aligning the red arrow with ˆz.
Number of Particles Needed.
The optimal location and amplitude of a cross-
polar current in the weather screen plane was identiﬁed earlier. The previous para-
graph identiﬁed problems when combining the solution for both polarization using
the particles labeled Px′ and Pz′. This paragraph intends to approximately quantify
the number of particles needed, where one particle is a reference to that given in
Fig. 5.13(a).
Figure 5.14 displays the single particle in WIPL-D with a plane wave incident
orthogonal to the particle. The red arrow indicates the orientation of the electric
ﬁeld. By using a ﬁeld strength approximately equal to that at the dipole locations
given in Table 5.3, the EFIE-MoM engine in WIPL-D may be used to estimate the
generated current in Fig. 5.13(b). In practice the setup of Fig. 5.14 has been used,
changing the polarization of the plane wave when evaluating the eﬀect of the particles
Px′ and Pz′. The dipole is constructed using a ﬂat PEC with a width of 0.15 mm.
The net dipole moment is approximately equal to the diﬀerence,
Inet = IPx′ −IPz′,
(5.21)
which when divided by the dipole moment identiﬁed early gives as estimate on the
number of particles needed:
Nparticles = Inet
I+
,
(5.22)
where I+ ≈643 µA m from Table 5.3. IPx′ and IPz′ are obtained by integrating the
surface current over the entire surface of the particle.
The excited current on the particle will depend on the dimension as shown in
Fig. 5.14, and a parameter study is performed varying the length in the co and cross-
polar direction (Lc and Lx respectively) in Fig. 5.15. The chosen length values are

Figure 5.15. Parameter study on the number of particles and the resulting phase.
based on the plot in Fig. 5.11 showing the phase of the current as a function of dipole
length. To achieve reasonable phase, it was earlier identiﬁed that the total length of
the dipole should not exceed 7.5 mm.
The number of dipoles are seen to decrease as the total length of the bend dipole
is increased. Unfortunately, the phase suﬀer substantially and as a consequence ef-
fectively limits the maximum lengths. The phase is calculated relative to the center
of incident ﬁeld, and the desired phase is thus closer to 90° than the 82° of Table 5.3
and the parameter study in Fig. 5.8. The lowest number of particles in this study is
estimated to about 10.
Given some distance between the particles, one particle is estimated to occupy an
area of 1 cm2. Given the area identiﬁed by the D. J. Bem analysis in Fig. 3.6 and
the rapid decreasing strength of the electric ﬁeld as an observer moves away from the
axis of the horn in Fig. 4.3, it is estimated that no more than 6 particles may ﬁt in
any reasonable way. The estimated number of particles needed exceeds 6, indicating
that a solution to both polarization using this approach is beyond reach.
The identiﬁed structure is not shown to be optimal, and possible other geometries
may do a better job.
This work will continue to investigate the idea of a screen
located in front of the horn, but only solving the problem for one polarization. That
is using only the particle as shown in Figs. 5.13(a) and 5.14 instead of the combined
shown in Fig. 5.13(b). The next chapter will assume a incident ﬁeld from the horn
being ˆv polarized.
Bandwidth Considerations.
This chapter has been concerned with only a single
frequency, whereas an actual implementation would be required to perform over the
entire transmit band. An implementation using a short dipole is expected not to be
frequency sensitive since the scatterer operates far from resonance. Performing over
the relative small band between 13.75 and 14.5 GHz should be achievable without
further justiﬁcation.
==PAGE:92==
72
CHAPTER 5. CURRENT AND PARTICLE DESIGN
5.4
Summary
This chapter has investigated the possibility and feasibility of reducing the cross
polarization radiation by inserting a 2D structure 5 mm in front of the horn. It has
been shown, that two dipoles located on either side of the symmetry plane inspired
by the focal plane ﬁeld distribution may mitigate the problem. The dipole moment
of the dipoles are about 650 µA m−1 and should be about 90° out of phase with the
incident ﬁeld from the horn. Given the strength of the electric ﬁeld at the location of
the dipoles, enough available power is present to achieve the needed dipole moment.
However, when the dipole is made short enough to achieve a signiﬁcant phase delay,
the dipole moment is reduced to about one third of what is needed for a ﬂat dipole.
The operation of the structure on both side of the symmetry plane was identiﬁed
as being a 90° ﬁeld rotation and 90° phase delay. The solution to one side is when
mirrored also a solution to the other. By using symmetry, chirality is shown to be a
necessary requirement for a 2D solution.
An implementation is proposed using a S shaped dipole. When two such S shaped
dipoles are combined, the structure should in principle generate the needed current
for an arbitrary incident ﬁeld.
However, the two particles are counteracting each
other, further aggregating the issue of exciting enough current. It is concluded that a
full solution is not possible using the S-shaped dipole, and that a further investigation
will be limited to a ˆv polarized source.
==PAGE:93==
6
Results and Full-Wave Simulation
This chapter present the results obtained from using the particle designed in the
previous chapter. Multiple particles aligned side by side forms the 2D screen in front
of the horn as displayed in Fig. 6.1.
6.1
Particle Optimization
Setup and Sittings
WIPL-D is used as EFIE-MoM solver excited by a sheet of electric and magnetic
currents extracted from the SWE source in GRASP. The procedure is analog to the
source ﬁelds used in PO_program and the same veriﬁcation procedure applies (see
Section 4.3). The excitation currents are extracted 1 mm closer to the aperture of the
horn (y′ = 39 mm), in order to be physically seperated from the plane of the particles.
Figure 6.2 displays a current sheet in gray located behind the particles constituting
the screen. The meshing in WIPL-D is automatic and depends on whether the result
has converged for a certain reference frequency. The reference frequency is chosen 1.5
times larger than the design frequency to ensure convergence.
Figure 6.3 displays a cluster of particles and the deﬁning variables. The interpre-
tation of Lx and Lc remain unchanged from the previous chapter and the width of the
particles are again 0.15 mm. Dc and Dx are the separation between particles along
the co (ˆx′) and cross-polar (ˆz′) directions, and Sc is a possible shift between two lines
of particles. Sc is not used when the screen consists of only one line of particles per
side. The coordinates (x′
c, z′
c) denotes the positioning of one half of the structure,
where the dotted line located at z′ = 0 represents the line of mirror symmetry in
which one half of the screen is mirrored to create the other.
(a) ˆx′ˆz′-plane
(b) ˆx′ˆy′-plane
(c) ˆy′ˆz′-plane

MATLAB Optimization
An optimization is set up in MATLAB much like that
of Chapter 5 with ga.m and followed by a local solver. The global optimizer allows
linear inequality constraints in the form of
Ax ≤b
(6.1)
where A is a coeﬃcient matrix, x is the control variables and b is a constant vector.
In practice, only one half of the screen is designed and then mirrored to the other.
In order for the designed half to strictly occupy space in that half, and to limit
the particles in overlapping in both the co and cross-polar direction, the following
inequalities are enforced:
0.3 ≤z′
c −Lx
2
(6.2)
0.2 ≥Lc
2 −Dc
(6.3)
0.2 ≥Lx −Dx
(6.4)
which are incorporated into the form of Eq. (6.1) with the unit of mm assumed by
the code. The constants 0.3 and 0.2 mm are chosen to leave a minimum of distance
between particles. Upper and lower bound are deﬁned on all control variables (I.e.
the particle dimensions) following Table 6.1. The limits are chosen after initial tests
with the dimension intervals quite narrow to lower convergence time.
The MATLAB script minimizes the far-ﬁeld cross-polar directivity in ﬁve φ-cuts
between zero and 90° for θ = ±2°. The dimensions and position of the particles are
the control variables. The main script is labeled PO_simulation_WIPL_grid and
roughly runs through the following steps:
1. Initialization of needed variables by running PO_program.m using the original
excitation of the horn without any current perturbations.
2. Export ﬁeld grid at y′=39 mm from GRASP, convert to current in a WIPL-D
format and load it manually in the WIPL-D project.
3. Deﬁne optimization sittings: Stopping criteria, bounds and constraints etc.
4. Call global solver with WIPL_simulation.m as objective function. The objec-
tive function performs the following tasks:
4.1. Modiﬁes the WIPL-D project ﬁle containing the symbol values (.smb) ac-
cording to the speciﬁc choices of control variables.
4.2. Executing the EFIE-MoM solver in WIPL-D by calling wipl.exe on the
symbolic project ﬁle (.IWS).
==PAGE:96==
76
CHAPTER 6. RESULTS AND FULL-WAVE SIMULATION
4.3. Loading of the resulting from the WIPL-D current ﬁle (.cu1) into MAT-
LAB according to the technical description in Appendix B.
5. A local optimizer fminsearch.m is called to further locate the found global min-
imum.
5.1. Repeats the procedure of the global optimizer above.
Note that fmin-
search.m does not abide by the constrains of Eqs. (6.2) to (6.4) or Ta-
ble 6.11.
6. Run WIPL_simulation.m once more using the best ﬁt.
7. Load and store the excited currents and log the far-ﬁeld response and the values
of the control variables.
Both MATLAB scripts are shown in Appendix D.6. One execution of the objective
function last between 40 s and 1.5 min on a regular desktop PC, and more than 600
runs are needed before a useful result is obtained.
Figure 6.4 displays the diﬀerent conﬁgurations used, varying from 1 to 6 particles.
By including a larger number of particles, the amount of current excited on the particle
in the desired direction should be increased. The particular plots shown corresponds
to the best result obtained, with the colors displaying the current density on the
particles.
1The implementation of the bend in WIPL-D sets certain minimum requirements to Lx and Lc,

approached a very small value.

6.2
Results
Initial Results.
The initial result are displayed in Table 6.2 and Fig. 6.5 for the
m1_2 conﬁguration only. Itotal indicates the integral of the current, with the super-
script indicating the current direction. The z′-directed current is expected to be close
to the calculated dipole moment of the previous chapter. ∠Iz′
total is the angle of the
current integral relative to the center of the E-ﬁeld, and is thus expected exhibit and
phase delay of 90°.
It is evident that the cross-polar level is reduced by about 8 dB from the uncom-
pensated case. Unfortunately, the reduction is followed by a decrease of Dco,max by
3.6 dB from 42.2 dB. Any decrease in the co-polar directivity is devastating, as it
eﬀects the signal to noise ratio directly.
The total dipole moment in the z′-direction is smaller than 650 µA m, and the
phase is far from 90°. Inspecting the particle shape in Fig. 6.5 it is clear, that the
bend is almost reduced to nothing. With the objective function only evaluating Dcx,
it is just as eﬀective simply to cancel the ﬁeld radiated by the horn, especially doing so
at the more curved part of the reﬂector. The lower directivity of the dipole compared
to the horn explains why the net result is a larger reduction in Dcx than in Dco. The
optimization algorithm has converged towards a high Ix′
total, and a phase diﬀerence
close to zero.
The dimensions of the particle in this conﬁguration are outside the upper and
lower limits deﬁned in Table 6.1, because of the local solver followed the gradient

The next subsection presents the results without the local solver, in order to force
a solution canceling the cross-polar radiation in the secondary pattern, and not just
the ﬁeld radiated by the horn.
Results from Global Solver.
Table 6.3 summaries the result using only the global
solver (ga.m), in terms of dipole moments and directivity. Table 6.3 lists the control
variables yielding the best ﬁt.
An improvement in the cross-polar directivity has been achieved, without sacri-
ﬁcing a signiﬁcant amount of co-polar directivity. The dipole moments, Iz′
total, are
surprisingly close to 650 µA m, and with a smaller current directed in the co-polar
direction compared to the initial results. Ix′
total is now maximum 1.4 mA m against
4 mA m before. The particles are thus better at directing currents in the orthogonal
direction.
The phase shifts are disappointing and explain why the best obtained reduction
is not larger than 6.4 dB. It is possible that a further improvement of a few dB could
be achieved if the optimization has been allowed to continue.
The mutual coupling between particles are included by the EFIE-MoM calculation,
and one could suspect the loading of nearby particles to lower the phase. But the
m1_1 conﬁguration does not show a larger phase shift even though only one particle
is present per side. The attempt to use more particles, and thus lower the total length

of the particles, seems to have failed. The conﬁgurations m2 and m3 has not achieved
a larger phase delay, and the total length (Lx + Lc) are not signiﬁcantly diﬀerent.
It is concluded that the lack of a 90° phase shift is the primary cause of failure.
The parameter study in Fig. 5.8(a) predicts a Dcx,max of about 9 dB using a 60° phase
delay. Extrapolating the plot, one would expect around 11 dB with ∠Iz′
total ≈50°, as
was achieved by the simulations.
The secondary radiation pattern of the m1_3 conﬁguration is shown in Fig. 6.6.
The dots representing the cross-polar requirements are modiﬁed by the slightly lower
co-polar directivity. The insertion of the screen has made the pattern comply with
the 30 dB cross-polarization discrimination requirements against only 25 dB before.
The result conﬁrms that it is possible to create a structure that performs the desired
operation for one polarization, under the assumption of zero mutual coupling between
the feed and particle screen.
Summary
A realizable particle has been tested using WIPL-D to calculate the currents based on
the ﬁelds from the original horn. The particle generates the desired cross-polar current
component but fails to achieve the phase delay needed. The expected performance
from the study of superimposed Hertzian dipoles are thus not obtained. The attempt
to achieve a more capacitative loading by using more particles did not work. The
screen has nevertheless improved the cross-polarization discrimination from 25 to
30 dB.
==PAGE:101==
6.3. FULL-WAVE SIMULATION
81
The next section will verify the calculations above simulations using only GRASP
and test the screens performance when mutual coupling is included.
6.3
Full-Wave Simulation
Throughout the project, it has been assumed that the screen could be designed disre-
garding the mutual coupling between the horn and the particles. As an intermediate
assumption, this has served well in investigating the dynamics at play, and how some
sort of structure could be designed and placed in front of the horn to mitigate the
problem in question. However, whether the assumption is justiﬁable has not been
investigated.
This section intends to get as close to a experimental veriﬁcation of the proposed
screen design as possible, by doing a full-wave simulation in GRASP, and thus includ-
ing the mutual coupling. By performing all calculating within GRASP, all possible
errors injected somewhere in the conversion process between GRASP, MATLAB and
WIPL-D are removed.
Setup.
The initial m1_2 instantiation with two particles on either side is selected
from the previous chapter and imported into GRASP using a tabulated mesh. The
structure is read from WIPL-D and output as a GRASP format (.msh) using the
script ReadWIPL_toGRASP.m in Appendix D.7.
The full geometry of the horn is imported from CHAMP, and a waveguide exci-
tation is added using the TE11 mode. Two setups are used:
1. Two seperate MoM objects, one for the horn and one for the 2D screen. The
surface current on the horn is evaluated based on the waveguide excitation, and
the particle current is subsequently evaluated based surface currents alone.
2. One combined MoM object containing both horn and screen. All currents are
calculated from the waveguide excitation.
The ﬁrst conﬁguration enables the same simulation as the optimization in the
beginning of this chapter, ignoring the mutual coupling between the horn and the
particles. The second includes all eﬀects and constitutes the full-wave simulation.
Results from Conﬁguration 1.
Fig. 6.7 displays the result comparing that of
Section 6.2, from MATLAB and WIPL-D, with that calculated by GRASP alone.
Good correspondence is seen with only minor discrepancies. All major features in
the GRASP simulation is correctly captured in the MATLAB calculations.
The
MATLAB and WIPL-D implementation is veriﬁed as correct, and the simulated data
are concluded reliable.
Recall that the source grid derived from the SWE source used in this work is only
approximative correct. All evanescent waves and near-ﬁeld eﬀects were lost in the
process. Performing a full EFIE-MoM of the horn was not intended at the outset of
the project, but may be used to verify the source grid. The radiated ﬁelds from the

Results from Conﬁguration 2
Figure 6.8 displays the result of the full-wave
simulation, comparing the results obtained with and without mutual coupling, both
calculated by GRASP alone. Agreements between the plots are scarce and all im-
provements in the cross-polar directivity are lost. The maximum now approaches
25 dB, a vast degradation in performance compared to the original system.
The return loss of the waveguide excitation gives a clue to the amount of coupling
between the horn the the particles. Without the presence of particles, the returnloss
is simulated to 33 dB. Including the particles, the value has dropped to 6 dB. Mutual
coupling is concluded to have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence, and a particle or screen cannot
be design disregarding the eﬀect.
From the full wave simulation it is clear, that the coupling back into the horn
has to be minimized or included in the optimization in order for the screen to work.
Including the coupling in the optimization seems cumbersome and time consuming,
with later modiﬁcations diﬃcult to handle.
Reducing the coupling between the particle and the horn could be achieved by
designing an asymmetric forward and backwards directivity (in the y′ positive and
negative direction respectively). Ideally, one would reduce the radiated ﬁeld towards
the horn to zero. This is not possible for a 2D structure but could be achieve by

Results from Conﬁguration 2 using the m1_3 Result
The result of a full-

between the plots with and without mutual coupling are signiﬁcant, but however less
than the example shown above. The return loss of the horn has only dropped to
17.3 dB. All improvement in the polarization discrimination are nonetheless lost.
Whether the lowered coupling is due to the smaller excited co-polar current or
the diﬀerent placement of the particles are speculative. It is noted that the m1_3
conﬁguration has one more particle per side than m1_2, and the particles in m1_3
are closer to the axis of the horn. Both fact that suggests a larger mutual coupling
and not smaller as seen here. In any case, the coupling either reduces the desired
cross-polar current or removed the capacitative eﬀect of the short dipoles.

6.4
Summary
The particle design of the last chapter has been tested in 6 diﬀerent conﬁguration,
with the number of particles varying from 1 to 6 on either side of the symmetry
plane.
WIPL-D has been used as EFIE-MoM solver with a "equivalent current"
representation of the feed, and MATLAB has been used to calculate the secondary
radiation pattern based on the particle currents.
The predicted dipole moment from the Hertzian dipole investigations was achieved
when the optimization algorithm was force to use particle dimensions in a narrow
range. The desired dipole moment was not achieved, and the obtained results suﬀer
as a consequence. An improvement of 6.4 dB was achieved enabling the antenna to
pass the 30 dB cross-polarization discrimination requirement.
The EFIE-MoM and secondary radiation pattern calculations have been veriﬁed
by simulations in GRASP, and the MATLAB and WIPL-D setup is concluded reliable.
A full-wave simulation is performed in GRASP, where the inﬂuence of mutual
coupling between the horn and the particles was quantiﬁed. Mutual coupling is seen
to have a large eﬀect, completely removing the positive eﬀect of the particle screen.
The performance including mutual coupling is seen to be worse than the orginal
by 7 dB in peak cross-polar directivity in one example. That a signiﬁcant mutual
coupling is present is further veriﬁed by the return loss of the horn, dropping from
33 dB to 6 dB and 17.3 dB in the examples.
It is concluded that the methodology followed in this work is questionable. Mutual
coupling poses a large problem and no 2D structure able to perform the desire rotation
and delay of the ﬁeld has been identiﬁed.
==PAGE:105==
7
Conclusion
The source of cross polarization in the secondary radiation pattern of a oﬀset reﬂector
antenna has been investigated. The source was identiﬁed as missing symmetry since
the curvature of the reﬂector inevitably generates a cross-polar current component.
The said component is out of phase on either side of the reﬂector, why the radiated
ﬁelds exactly cancel in the symmetry plane.
The problem may be mitigated by increasing the focal length of the parent
paraboloid but results in large bulky antennas. An analysis of the electric ﬁeld dis-
tribution in the focal plane is performed, and suggests locations in which currents
may be superimposed. The secondary radiation from the currents are to cancel the
cross-polar component generated by the reﬂector.
In investigation the problem, this project has used the geometry and feed of the
EXPLORER 8100 by Cobham SATCOM. The antenna has a weather protecting
screen located 5 mm in front of the horn aperture. The plane of the weather screen
serves as the location for the superimposed currents. A MATLAB program is devel-
oped which calculates the secondary radiation pattern using physical optics based on
an arbitrary current distribution in the focal region. The MATLAB code is tested
extensively against GRASP and correctly predicts the secondary radiation.
By imitating a conjugate match to the focal plane electric ﬁeld using two or more
superimposed currents, it is shown that the cross polarization may be suppressed by
at least 11 dB. The result applies for two orthogonal polarizations of feed, represented
by equivalent currents at 14.125 GHz.
For the two dipole conﬁguration, the dipoles are located symmetrically about
the symmetry plane with a dipole moment of approximately 650 µA m, and oriented
in opposite directions along the cross-polar axis of the source ﬁeld. The phase of
should be 90° delayed relative to the ﬁeld of the horn. The operation of the dipoles
corresponds to turning parts of the ﬁeld 90° to either side depending on location and
delaying the ﬁeld by the same amount.
A solution using dipoles as scatterers has been investigated.
It is shown that
enough available power is present in the driving ﬁeld to achieve the desired dipole
moment. A short dipole results in a capacitative loading and may approach the needed
phase delay, while the geometry accounts for the change in polarization. However,
the more capacitative the dipole, the lower is the excited surface current due to a
increasing mismatch.
The dipole is modiﬁed with an orthogonal bend in the middle, and the number
of dipoles needed are investigated based on the excited current from a plane wave.
It is concluded that the found structure may not work for both polarizations, but
an implementation of only one polarization is pursued to investigate the feasibility of
providing the conjugate match in front of the horn.
Multiple instantiations of the bend dipole is tested using WIPL-D as a EFIE-MoM
85
==PAGE:106==
86
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSION
solver, based on the actual ﬁelds from the horn not including mutual coupling. The
dimensions of the dipoles are identiﬁed using an optimization routine in MATLAB,
while the number of dipoles are varied from 1 to 6 on either side of the symmetry
plane. The predicted dipole moment is achieved but only with a 50° phase delay. The
performance suﬀer as a result, with the best improvement in cross-polar directivity
being 6.4 dB using 4 bend dipoles on both sides. The cost is a reduction in co-polar
directivity by 0.5 dB.
When mutual coupling is included by performing a full-wave simulation over the
combined horn and the screen in GRASP, the reduction completely disappears. The
dipole conﬁguration could be made more directive by expanding into three dimen-
sions, but even in that case, the found solution only applies to one polarization. It
is concluded that the methodology followed in this work is questionable.
Mutual
coupling poses a large problem and no two dimensional structure is identiﬁed able to
perform the desire rotation and phase delay.
7.1
Future work
As stated in the above, the methodology followed has not been successful, and not
shown a suitable direction for further work. One could investigate the eﬀect of moving
the screen further from the horn to reduce the coupling issue, but even in that case no
full solution is identiﬁed. It was further shown in Section 5.1 that results are better
close to the focal point when using two Hertzian dipoles.
An alternative and possibly better approach is to look at the ﬁeld properties at the
dipole locations, and design a solution for both polarizations directly. The operation
of the inserted dipoles or particles in the screen was in Section 5.2 identiﬁed as a
rotation and delay of the incident ﬁeld. It is a well known fact that two opposite
rotation circular polarizations wave could have been chosen instead of the two linear
polarization used throughout this work.
Denoting ˆR and ˆL as the right and left hand polarized unit vectors respectively
in terms of a vertical and horizontal component in a column vector:

The 10° was selected randomly. The property of unequal conversion of circular po-
larized waves is known as circular conversion dichroism [31].
[32] includes a practical realization of a 2D screen displaying the desired property.
Unfortunately, the property is inherently linked to lossy materials [31, Ch. 3], [32], a
serious issue for a application sensitive to any insertion loss. In other words, a lossless
implementation may be impossible and may explain why this work has not arrived
at a proper solution to the problem.
An implementation of a meta metamaterial into the oﬀset reﬂector application
may be diﬃcult. The design of metasurfaces are assumed of inﬁnity extent in order
to incorporate mutual coupling between units cells. As identiﬁed in this thesis, an
application here require two very concentrated and closely spaced regions of diﬀerent
properties.
Utilizing frequency dependent materials also reintroduces the issue of
bandwidth otherwise avoided by the small dipoles.
Nevertheless, the author recommends that any future work should be carried out
looking at the speciﬁc properties of a screen located at the cross-polar peak of the
focal plane ﬁeld. The instantiation in [32] displaying the rotational property is not
obvious and does not show 4-fold symmetry, and thus would not have been identiﬁed
following the approach outlined in this work.

==PAGE:114==
B
Import of Currents from WIPL-D
WIPL-D does not provide an easy way of exporting the excited currents on scatterers,
even though the currents are calculated. This script reads the internal WIPL-D ﬁles
and exports the currents of a 2D structure as used in this project (the script needs
modiﬁcation to work with an arbitrary 3D structures). The technical description that
follows are based on [22, Sec. 4.1.1.2].
The WIPL-D project ﬁle (.IWP) stores a list of all notes and plates in the project,
with the notes given by a ID number and a set of coordinates in (x, y, z), and the
plates given by the four note IDs indicating its vertices. A separate ﬁle (.cu1) stores
the information of the currents on each plate, given by coordinate of p and s in
components of ˆip and ˆis. The ﬁle formats are speciﬁed in [22].
Plates in WIPL-D are given in a very general form: The nonplaner quadrilateral
uniquely deﬁned by its four vertices. This is shown in Fig. B.1 where r11, r12, r21,
and r22, are the position vectors of its vertices (I.e., the coordinates of the notes), and
p1, p2, s1, and s2 are arbitrary starting and ending coordinates of the quadrilateral
sides in the local coordinate system adopted.
Figure B.1. The plate representation used in WIPL-D is that of a nonplaner quadri-
latereal, which is uniquely deﬁned by its four vertices. Illustration from [22, Sec.
4.1.1.2].
WIPL-D addopts a convention with −p1 = p2 = −s1 = s2 = 1, and thus stores
currents in rectangular grid with p ∈[1; −1] and s ∈[1; −1] in a speciﬁed resolution
depending on the used reference frequency. The reference frequency is chosen such
that the results converges, and may be chosen higher or lower than the highest fre-
quency of the simulation. In this work, a factor of 1.5 is used to ensure a converged
result. Note that the output currents has nothing to do with the basis functions used
in the EFIE-MoM calculation.
Even though the coordinate system of ˆip and ˆis are square, the plate do not need
to be. That further entails that ˆip and ˆis may not be directly converted to the (x,y)

system. Figure B.2(a) displays a plate in relative to a ordinary coordinate system
(x, y). It is seen that ˆip and ˆis change direction depending on which coordinate (p,s)
is evaluated. For each (s,p) coordinate, the coordinate in (x,y) is calculated as
r(p, s) =1

(B.2)
Figure B.2(b) displays the plate with a small square highlighted. The small square
has 4 corners deﬁned in (p,s) coordinates, and by using Eq. (B.2) we may convert all
corners to (x,y). The direction of ˆip and ˆis are relative to these coordinates and are
calculated in (x,y) components as
ˆip = n1 −n3,
(B.3)
ˆis = n1 −n2,
(B.4)
for the corner of the small square labeled n1. The current surface density components
are then found as the dot product
Ix = ˆx ·

ˆipIp +ˆisIs


(B.5)

ˆipIp +ˆisIs

.
(B.6)
The desired quantity is the current or dipole moment to input into PO_program
at each square with the center coordinate given by
nsquare = n1 + n2 + n3 + n4
4
.
(B.7)
==PAGE:116==
B3
The current density is then assumed constant over the square and given by the mean
four corners
Isquare = I1 + I2 + I3 + I4
4
,
(B.8)
where I1 through I4 are the current densities at each coordinate. When the number
of small plates representing the plate is large, the representation should mirror the
physical behavior.
The current moment is found by multiplying by the area of the small square
Asquare
Imoment = AsquareIsquare,
(B.9)
and Asquare is calculated as the determinant of the parallelogram deﬁned by 2 vectors.
The vectors are found from the 4 vertices, n1 through n4, and the area becomes half
of the sum of two determinants:
Asquare = 1
2
h det(n1 −n2 n1 −r3)

(B.10)
+
det(n4 −n2 n4 −n3)

i
.
(B.11)
The absolute value is applied since the result of the determinant may be negative but
of the correct magnitude.
The practical implementation of the determinant is only valid when all notes are
in the same plane, which is the case for this project. Modifying the implementation
of Eq. (B.11) to work for an arbitrary oriented plate should be fairly easy.

D.3
Veriﬁcation Scripts
Veriﬁcation scripts are used to verify or test the behavior of PO_program.m, and are
commented out as they serve no purpose during regular executions. To select them,
uncomment the line in which the script is called. The scripts are listed below including
a description of each, and afterwards displayed in full length using MATLAB syntax.
1. calculate_Prad.m
Calculates the total radiated power by the feed alone, by integrating over the a
closed sphere. Integration over the source grid is performed as a trial with an
identical result.
2. TestScriptHertzianDipole.m
Script to insert Hertzian dipoles instead of the source grid. The far-ﬁeld radi-
ation pattern may then be calculated and compared to GRASP. A Huygens’
source is constrcuted using dipoles and also found in the script.
3. TestScriptMetasurfaceGrid.m
Test script to test the source grid alone. Includes tests of diﬀerence ﬁeld cuts
to test the eﬀect of using the "SWE to PWE to grid"-procedure.
The ﬁnal
veriﬁcation is obtained testing the far-ﬁeld radiation to that of the SWE source
from GRASP.

==PAGE:149==
D28
APPENDIX D. MATLAB SCRIPTS AND FUNCTIONS
D.4
Dipole Optimization Functions
Optimization scripts are used whenever the MATLAB optimization toolbox is called.
The main script is called PO_optimization.m and runs a setup section that initiates
all variables and calculates the far-ﬁeld radiation of the source alone. In doing this
the PO_program.m is called, which must have the variable PO_optimization.m set
to true.
Furthermore, the orientation of the ﬁeld, grid resolution etc.
are set in
PO_program.m. The variable E_xz_horn_exitation_angle speciﬁcally controls the
feed polarization with the values 90 og 0 corresponds to ˆv and ˆh respectively. All
other values are invalid and will return an error.
A number of optimization subroutines are available, and must be called by ex-
ecuting diﬀerent sections of PO_optimization.m. The subroutines superpositions a
current in the focal region and calculates the combined far-ﬁeld as a superposition
of that of the feed and the superimposed current.
All calculations after the ini-
tial setup of the currents are common for all subroutines and are thus gathered in
PO_optimization_func_loopsection.m, and are essentially the same as the later part
of PO_program.m but without a magnetic current.
All optimization functions are listed below with a short description.
1. PO_optimization_func_2dipoles_free.m
Two dipoles located symmetrically at (x′, y′, ±z′) with the free variables of x′,
z′ amplitude and phase of each dipoles.
2. PO_optimization_func
Two dipoles located symmetrically at (x′, y′, ±z′) with with the same but op-
posite dipole moment.
3. PO_optimization_func_ratio.m
Same as 2, but with the amplitude and phase deﬁned by the local electric ﬁeld
at the position where the dipole are located.
The label ratio is due to the
amplitude being limited by a ratio to the local E-ﬁeld.
4. PO_optimization_func_4dipoles_ﬁxed.m
Same as 2, but with four dipoles instead of two.
All scripts and functions are listed in the full length using MATLAB syntax below.
For further explanation, refer to the optimization function description in Chapter 5.
../PO_optimization.m

D.6
WIPL-D Simulation scripts
This section includes the script used in the optimization the particle dimensions. A de-
tailed description is given in Chapter 6 and not repeated here. PO_simulation_WIPL_grid.m
sets up the simulations analog to PO_optimization.m and WIPL_simulation.m is the
function to be optimized which calls the WIPL-D EFIE-MoM solver and subsequently
loads the results for processing.

D.7
Compatibility scripts
A number of scripts has been necessary to transfer information between MATLAB,
GRASP and WIPL-D. All but one has been written speciﬁcally for this project. The
are listed below with a corresponding explanation.
1. ReadGRASP_grdﬁle.m
The script reads grid ﬁles (.grd) from GRASP into MATLAB from a TICRA
speciﬁc format outlined in [19]. Note that the length unit of the grid is not
speciﬁed in the grid, but only given inside the GRASP project.
The script
assumes the unit of mm.
==PAGE:180==
D.7. COMPATIBILITY SCRIPTS
D59
2. ReadWIPL_ﬁles.m
Reads the currents from a WIPL-D project, and is described in detail in Ap-
pendix B. This particular script only calculates the total dipole moment of the
project, but a modiﬁed version is included in WIPL_simulation.m where the
actual positioning of each current moment in the (x, y, z) coordinate system is
crucial.
3. ReadWIPL_toGRASP.m
Loads the 2D structure of a WIPL-D and outputs the structure in GRASP
format to be loaded in as a tabulated mesh. The function assigns a predeﬁned
coordinate for the third dimension and will return an erroneous result if a
3D structure is used as input. The script is used to input the optimized real
implementation of the solution into GRASP to perform a full-wave simulation
without having to use the very cumbersome structure editor of GRASP.
4. ReadGRASP_cutﬁle.m
This script reads the .cut ﬁles from GRASP containing plotting values of for
instance directivity as a function of angle. This particular script is not written
by the author but an old script by the company supervisor Ulrich Gothelf.
../ReadGRASP_grdﬁle.m
